** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA HERNANDEZ-RODRIGUEZ
File: AXXX XX6 220 - Dallas, TX
October 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark Lee, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On February 29, 2012, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the June 25, 2010, decision in which the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had abandoned her application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), for failure to file the application in a timely manner. On June 5, 2012, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen in which she alleges ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of her former attorney. The Department of Homeland Security has not opposed the motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She has also offered evidence in support of an application for adjustment of status that reveals her prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status as the parent of a United States citizen at the time of the removal hearing. Upon consideration of the evidence before us, it appears that the respondent suffered prejudice as a result of her former attorney’s handling of her case. Id. at 640.
 
Although the respondent’s motion is untimely by 6 days, in light of the particular circumstances present in this case, the Board will exercise its sua sponte authority and grant this unopposed motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the proceedings will be reopened and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s application for adjustment of status, and for other action as may be deemed appropriate.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5473671 (BIA)

2012 WL 5473677 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NEVILLE COOPSAMMY A.K.A. MOOSANM COOPSAMMY A.K.A. MOOSAM COOPSAMMY A.K.A. NEVILLE MOONSAN A.K.A. INDIO COOPSAMMY A.K.A. NEVILLE COOBSAMMY’
File: AXXX XX0 264 - New York, NY
October 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriel J. Marous, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On April 20, 2012, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent subject to removal, denying his request for a continuance, and ordering him removed from the United States. On May 14, 2012, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. He later supplemented the motion with additional evidence. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a reply to the motion and the motion is therefore deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3).1 The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented the respondent before the Immigration Judge and on appeal to the Board and who also filed the visa petitions (Form I-130) for the respondent’s adult United States citizen son on behalf of the respondent. The respondent asserts that the former attorney mishandled the visa petition process, and that because of his former attorney’s errors, the petitions were denied, leading to the respondent’s inability to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent has submitted documents that he states would have supported the visa petitions. The respondent has also submitted evidence showing that his adult United States citizen daughter has recently filed a visa petition on his behalf. The respondent seeks adjustment of status, in conjunction with a waiver of inadmissibility, based on the pending visa petition.
 
Given the lack of opposition to reopening from the DHS, the timeliness of the motion, and the previously unavailable evidence that the respondent’s United States citizen daughter filed a Form I-130 on the respondent’s behalf after the respondent’s last hearing in this matter, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings on the respondent’s application for adjustment of status. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988).2 In light of our decision in this matter, we need not address whether the respondent has established that he received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

1

It appears the respondent originally moved to reopen with the Immigration Court. The DHS filed a statement of opposition to that motion because jurisdiction was with this Board.

2

The parties are responsible for notifying the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit about this decision.

IN RE: NEVILLE COOPSAMMY A.K.A. MOOSANM COOPSAMMY A.K.A. MOOSAM COOPSAMMY A.K.A. NEVILLE MOONSAN A.K.A. INDIO COOPSAMMY A.K.A. NEVILLE COOBSAMMY’
File: AXXX XX0 264 - New York, NY
October 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriel J. Marous, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On April 20, 2012, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent subject to removal, denying his request for a continuance, and ordering him removed from the United States. On May 14, 2012, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. He later supplemented the motion with additional evidence. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a reply to the motion and the motion is therefore deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3).1 The motion will be granted.

The respondent has submitted documents that he states would have supported the visa petitions. The respondent has also submitted evidence showing that his adult United States citizen daughter has recently filed a visa petition on his behalf. 2012 WL 5473633 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADRIAN KRISHNA BADALOO
File: AXXX XX8 408 - Miami, FL
October 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kenneth Panzer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Vera Hochberg
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Jamaica. An in absentia order of removal was first entered against him on December 8, 2011. On December 21, 2011, the respondent filed a motion seeking to rescind the court’s order. The motion to reopen was denied, and a motion for reconsideration followed on January 3, 2012. On January 4, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied reconsideration. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s statement that he wanted his day in court and did not understand the last decision. Given the respondent’s statements, the Immigration Judge denied reconsideration. We agree with the denial. We acknowledge that when the respondent filed his last motion, he submitted a hand-written statement on his own because his attorney was out of the country. Nonetheless, his motion failed to address the issues in the Immigration Judge’s decision with which he took issue. The federal regulations provide that a motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). See also Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). As the respondent’s statements did not meet this standard, the respondent did not establish a sufficient basis to justify reconsideration. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
We acknowledge that on appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge applied the wrong factors in evaluating the merits of his motion to reopen and he believes an in absentia order of removal was entered prematurely without affording him an opportunity to present any evidence (Br. at 2). These contentions relate to the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen-- arguments which more properly should have been advanced in the motion for reconsideration and not on appeal now. To the extent that the respondent also submits affidavits from family and friends and a letter written to his former attorney of record, Ms. Sharon Brown, to establish that he was not properly advised by Ms. Brown, these documents, again, should have been introduced earlier.1 We conclude that the respondent failed to establish a legal or factual basis for reconsideration by the Immigration Judge and specifically, as to the merits of the appeal, we agree with the government that this appeal is not an opportunity for the respondent to file new evidence or advance contentions that should have been made at a prior stage (DHS Br. at 3).
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In spite of the respondent’s attorney’s dissatisfaction wtih Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637,639 (BIA 1988), as a matter of law, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim will not lie, if the requirements in Lozada are not met.

2012 WL 5473635 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUZ MARILU PERDOMO-MERCADO
File: AXXX XX1 731 - E1 Paso, TX
October 22, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Benjamin T. Wiesinger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorely R. Fernandez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The pending motion to reopen, which seeks to reopen and rescind the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2005, decision ordering the respondent removed in absentia, is both untimely filed and number barred and has not been shown to satisfy any exception to the filing requirements imposed by law on motions to reopen removal proceedings. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7).
 
Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable filing deadline based on the respondent’s alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel(s) claim. The respondent contends that former counsel(s) failed to properly pursue her alleged lack of notice of her hearing claim based on her assertion that the address listed on the Notice to Appear does not correctly reflect the address that she provided and, as a result, the notice of hearing was sent to the wrong address. However, the respondent’s claim is undermined by her husband’s affidavit wherein he indicates that the Notice to Appear reflects the address that was provided by the respondent (Submission of Respondent’s Additional Evidence dated December 15, 2011, at 4). Despite the respondent’s assertion that first former counsel forged her signature on the March 3, 2010, dated affidavit that was submitted along with her first motion to reopen that was filed before the Immigration Judge on April 15, 2010, she does not claim that subsequent counsel forged her husband’s signature. Under the circumstances, we do not find the respondent’s assertion alone sufficient to demonstrate that the notice of hearing was sent to the wrong address. The respondent therefore has not sufficiently shown patent error or prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Nor has she substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against each of her former counsels. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
Finally, the respondent, who does not dispute personal service of the Notice to Appear, has not provided a coherent and persuasive explanation for why she waited almost five years to file a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. Nor has she demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reopening in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority.
 
*2 On this record, the respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the fact that her motion is both untimely filed and number barred. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5473635 (BIA)

2012 WL 5473646 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO GUTIERREZ A.K.A. LEONARDO GUITERREZ FLORES
File: AXXX XX9 223 - San Antonio, TX
October 18, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Venezuela. The Board dismissed his appeal on March 21, 2011, and denied a motion to reopen on April 25, 2012. The current motion was untimely filed on September 14, 2012, and is barred by numerical limitations on motions. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion to reopen, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks to have the Board reopen proceedings under its sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a). This action is taken only in “exceptional situations.” Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). This is not such an exceptional situation.
 
The respondent again makes a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge. We already considered the respondent’s claims against former counsel in our April 25, 2012, decision. His main claim against this attorney is that he failed to timely apply for withholding of removal before the Immigration Judge. As we stated in previously denying reopening, the respondent fails to show prejudice concerning this claim, DeZavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004), as he has not set forth any kind of persecution claim.
 
The respondent also now suggests that former counsel did not tell the Immigration Judge that he planned to marry and possibly seek adjustment of status on this basis. According to the respondent, he married on July 26, 2011. He also contends that his wife is asthmatic and will suffer hardship if he is removed. This is no basis for reopening of these proceedings, where there is nothing to suggest that the respondent’s wife has even filed a visa petition on his behalf.
 
The pending motion is, therefore denied, and the motion to change venue is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5473646 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 5473650 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS YAEL MORENO-AVELAR
File: AXXX XX1 674 - Oakdale, LA
October 17, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Brett Pouncey, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lorraine L. Griffin
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; voluntary departure
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has filed a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision issued July 3, 2012, which found the respondent removable and denied his request for a continuance and for voluntary departure. The respondent has not filed a brief on appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a response to the appeal requesting summary affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision; however, we do not find that summary affirmance would be appropriate in this case. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The factual findings of the Immigration Judge are reviewed to determine whether they are “clearly erroneous.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). All other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges, including legal and discretionary determinations and applications of law to fact, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); see also Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We find no clear error in the factual findings of the Immigration Judge in this case. Upon de novo review of the questions of law, discretion, and judgment presented, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance and voluntary departure.
 
In particular, the respondent foiled to establish good cause for a continuance in his case, where a request for the exercise of prosecutorial discretion had already been denied by DHS, the respondent was believed to be affiliated with a gang, and his conviction had not been vacated and remained valid for immigration purposes (I.J. at 1-9). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355 (BIA 1983). Moreover, the respondent has failed to show prejudice in his case. To date he has not presented any evidence that he has been granted prosecutorial discretion or deferred action by DHS, that he is not affiliated with a gang, or that his conviction has been vacated.1
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that he was denied due process. The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the telephonic connection at the last hearing was poor (I.J. at 2-3), but following mailing of a transcript of all hearings to counsel for the respondent, he has not filed a brief or made any further arguments in this regard.2 The respondent received a full and fair hearing in this case (I.J. at 1-9; Tr. at 1-14).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly noted that the respondent chose not to pursue pre-conclusion voluntary departure because he was not willing to waive appeal, and then properly denied post-conclusion voluntary departure due to the respondent’s conviction within 5 years (I.J. at 8). See sections 240B(a) and (b), 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229c(a) and (b); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(b) and (c).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has also not established that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his criminal proceedings and that such would have changed the outcome.

2

Counsel also represented the respondent below.

2012 WL 5473615 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZIYAAD SABAN A.K.A. SABAN ZIYAAD A.K.A. SABAN ZEYAAD
File: AXXX XX3 999 - Miami, FL
October 9, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Leslie Snyder, Esquire
 
CHARGES:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)] - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of South Africa who adjusted his status to that of a lawful permanent resident on March 16, 1999. On December 3, 2002, the respondent was placed in removal proceedings by the issuance of a Notice to Appear based on his convictions for burglary of an occupied dwelling and grand theft in the third degree under Fla. Stat. §§ 810.02(3)(a) and 812.014(2)(c), respectively. On February 11, 2005, an Immigration Judge entered an order of removal in absentia.
 
On April 19, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. In the motion, the respondent argued that based on Matter of Alyazji, 25 I&N Dec. 397 (BIA 2011), he was not removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) for a crime involving moral turpitude because he committed the theft and burglary offenses more than 5 years after his 1990 admission. He also argued that he should be permitted to re-enter the United States as a lawful permanent resident because the Notice to Appear was improvidently issued. The Department of Homeland Security opposed the motion to reopen as untimely.
 
On May 17, 2011, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. Relying on 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) and Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, 24 I&N Dec. 646 (BIA 2008), the Immigration Judge concluded that she did not have jurisdiction to reopen the respondent’s in absentia order of removal because the respondent departed the United States. The respondent now appeals. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2012). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings regarding witness credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i)(2012). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
*2 After the Immigration Judge issued her order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, decided Jian Le Lin v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 681 F.3d 1236 (11th Cir. 2012), in which the court disagreed with our holding in Matter of Armendarez-Mendez, supra. The court held that the regulatory departure bar, which prohibits the filing of a motion to reopen by or on behalf of a person who is the subject of removal proceedings subsequent to his or her departure from the United States, conflicts with the statutory right of an alien to file one motion to reopen. Jian Le Lin v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra, at 1239-41. See also section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(d), 1003.23(b). Thus, under the controlling authority of the Eleventh Circuit, the Immigration Judge had jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s motion to reopen, notwithstanding the respondent’s departure from the United States. Jian Le Lin v. U.S. Atty. Gen., supra. See Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25 (BIA 1989) (holding that the Board generally must follow circuit court precedent in cases arising in the circuit).
 
Nonetheless, we will affirm the denial of the motion to reopen on alternative grounds. A motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). A motion to reopen in an absentia removal order pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Act may be filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive notice of the hearing. Id.
 
The Immigration Judge entered the respondent’s in absentia removal order on February 11, 2005; the respondent filed his motion to reopen on April 19, 2011. The motion to reopen was untimely because the respondent filed it more than 180 days after the entry of the removal order. Further, the respondent does not allege, or establish, that his failure to appear was due to extraordinary circumstances as that term is defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). He also does not claim that he did not have notice of the February 11, 2005, hearing. Id. Indeed, the respondent’s motion to reopen indicates that his attorney appeared at the February 11, 2005, hearing although the respondent had already departed the United States (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2; Exh. 4). Based on the record before us, we conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen his in absentia order of removal was untimely.
 
*3 The respondent states in his motion and on appeal that he relied on erroneous advice of counsel and departed the United States (Respondent’s Br. at 7; Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2). To the extent that this statement may be construed as a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Matter of Compean, Bangaly, and J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009).
 
For these reasons we affirm the denial of the motion to reopen, albeit for reasons different from the Immigration Judge. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5473615 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 5473620 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BARBARA ROBINSON
File: AXXX XX2 823 - Buffalo, NY
October 5, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent has filed an untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). She seeks equitable tolling due to ineffective assistance of one of her former counsel, Daniel Aidif, who did not file a motion to reopen prior to expiration of the time within which she had to voluntarily depart.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Romero v. United States INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005). However, the respondent has not demonstrated a basis for equitable tolling as she has not shown prejudice. Vartelas v. Holder, 620 F.3d 108, 115 (2d Cir. 2010). That is, an alien claiming that she received ineffective assistance of counsel must show that counsel’s “performance was so ineffective as to have impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing.” Debeatham v. Holder, 602 F.3d 481, 485 (2d Cir. 2010)(citations omitted); Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 130 (2d Cir. 2008).
 
The respondent has not made this showing. The Immigration Judge entered the final administrative order in this case on August 5, 2003. The respondent did not enter into a contract with Mr. Aidif until November 17, 2003, which was after expiration of the 90 day period within which to file a timely motion to reopen. Therefore, Mr. Aidif could not have filed a timely motion to reopen or otherwise complied with Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002).
 
Further, the respondent has the burden to demonstrate that she exercised due diligence during the “entire” time she seeks to have tolled, which “includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed.” Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008). See also Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517 (2d Cir. 2007)(alien must demonstrate that he exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006)(“[N]o matter how egregiously ineffective counsel’s assistance may have been, an alien will not be entitled to equitable tolling unless he can affirmatively demonstrate that he exercised reasonable due diligence during the time period sought to be tolled.”).
 
*2 The respondent has not provided a sufficient explanation as to why she waited until 2012 to file the present motion regarding Mr. Aidif. The respondent has filed six previous motions, but she has not specifically alleged ineffective assistance as to Mr. Aidif, or otherwise satisfied the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, prior to this motion, even though the representation about which she complains occurred in 2003. Therefore, due diligence has not been demonstrated.1
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen shall be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that, hand-written on a Form EOIR-27 submitted with the present motion, the respondent also states that her “most recent lawyer, Andrew Rappeport,” did not present the new evidence in connection with a motion filed in 2011. However, no further explanation of this claim has been made, nor is there any evidence of Lozada compliance in connection with any claim against this attorney.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BARTOLO JEOVANI LOPEZ DE LEON
File: AXXX XX3 133 - Miami, FL
October 5, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Karen M. Iezzi, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before us on July 18, 2012, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order entered in his proceedings on January 15, 2008, and denied his subsequent motion to reopen. On August 17, 2012, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion and stay will denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (providing that a motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority”); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Here, the respondent asserts that the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s determination that his motion to reopen was time and number-barred (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 4-6). Specifically, the respondent asserts that his failure to appear for his hearing was due to both lack of notice and exceptional circumstances, i.e. ineffective assistance of a notario and thus, the time and number limits should be equitably tolled (Id.).
 
First, the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this matter arises, does not recognize equitable tolling of the time period for the filing of motions to reopen where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel has been made, but has held that the time period for filing motions to reopen is mandatory and jurisdictional. Ali Abdi v. U.S. Attorney General, 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2005); Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999). Moreover, we are not persuaded by the cases the respondent’s relies upon outside of this jurisdiction, or the arguments relating to an unpublished decision from the Eleventh Circuit. Thus, the respondent’s motion convinces of us no error in our determination that his motion to reopen was both time and number-barred. To the extent the respondent raises additional issues regarding the Immigration Judge’s and Board’s decision, those issues should have been raised on appeal or in his subsequent motion to reopen. See O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “[a] motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied” and “arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.”) (citation omitted). While the respondent may disagree with our prior decision, the motion does not provide any basis to conclude error in the Board’s ultimate decision. Accordingly, the motion and stay will be denied.1
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent is not precluded from addressing the request for the exercise of prosecutorial discretion or a stay of removal to DHS.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDUARDO MERCADO-CARLOS A.K.A. EDUARDO MERCADO A.K.A. EDWARDO MERCADO
File: AXXX XX1 574 - Chicago, IL
October 3, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Al Kola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brendan Curran
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 19, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal convictions.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. _, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a writ of habeas corpus to vacate it. The respondent has offered evidence of that with the appeal. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
The Immigration Judge granted the respondent continuances from March 2, 2012, until June 19, 2012, to allow the respondent to collaterally attack the conviction. The Immigration Judge declined to grant further continuance, noting that the writ was not filed until June 13, 2012.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
*2 While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel.

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

IN RE: JUAN ARMANDO LUA-MOTO
File: AXXX XX2 112 - Chicago, IL
October 2, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerardo S. Gutierrez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lynn K. Hollander
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 6, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent alleged in his motion to reopen that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel or submit sufficient documentation supporting his claim that he is eligible to pursue cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (requiring that a motion to reopen be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documents). Furthermore, we find no merit to the respondent’s arguments on appeal, where there is no evidence that a motion for a continuance was denied or that the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. In addition, the respondent has not presented any evidence demonstrating that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that he is inadmissible to the United States. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(c) (placing the burden on the alien to establish that he is clearly and beyond a doubt entitled to be admitted to the United States). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann

2012 WL 5473631 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO ANTONIO CORTES FERNANDEZ A.K.A. GUERNO HERNANDEZ CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX0 965 - Los Angeles, CA
October 2, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James F. Kajtoch, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final order of removal in this matter was entered on June 3, 2010, when this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent not eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). On August 6, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
With certain exceptions, an alien is entitled to file only one motion to reopen and the motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion is untimely.
 
The respondent asserts, however, that his motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and a claim that equitable tolling applies. A motion to reopen claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must substantially comply with the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. Prejudice results when the inadequate performance may have affected the outcome of the case. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785 (9th Cir. 2005).
 
The respondent has submitted evidence that after the Board’s decision was issued on June 3, 2010, he entered into an agreement with Michael J. Hernandez for the filing of a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit (Mot. at 1-3; Mot. Exh. A). The respondent has submitted a receipt showing that he paid the amount required for the filing (Mot. Exh. A). The respondent states that Mr. Hernandez advised him that the case would take a long time (Mot. at 2-3). The respondent reports that Mr. Hernandez did not file anything on the respondent’s behalf and that he was suspended from the practice of law by the Florida Bar at the time he entered into the agreement with the respondent to file the petition for review (Mot. at 2-3; Mot. Exh. B). The respondent also states that he now believes the attorney should have filed a motion to reconsider with the Board. The respondent reports that he has obtained the services of a criminal attorney, who specializes in post-conviction relief, to assist him with his criminal conviction, and that once the conviction is addressed, the respondent will be eligible for relief from removal. The respondent has submitted a copy of a visa petition (Form I-130) and evidence that his wife is a United States citizen (Mot. Exh. D).
 
*2 Accepting the respondent’s allegations concerning his former attorney, Michael J. Hernandez, it is extremely unfortunate that the respondent was apparently cheated out of a substantial amount of money. However, the respondent has not made any allegation that the result in his case might have been different if Mr. Hernandez had filed the promised petition for review, or in the alternative filed a motion to reconsider with the Board. It was determined during proceedings that the respondent was not eligible for the relief he sought, based on his controlled substance conviction. The respondent makes no challenge to the findings of the Board or the Immigration Judge. Thus, even though the Board may consider, as a matter of discretion, claims of ineffective assistance based on conduct that occurred after the final order of removal was entered, the respondent has not shown that this is a case that warrants reopening. See Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009).
 
To the extent the respondent speculates that his criminal conviction might be vacated in the future, this is not related to his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and is not a basis for reopening. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992) (providing that motions to reopen must establish prima facie eligibility for relief).
 
Finally, the respondent has submitted a supplemental filing with evidence concerning his work-place injury on April 19, 2006. He states that the injury was the subject of a workers compensation claim. He also makes references to a “U Visa” application in relationship to the claim, but he does not explain how any such application would be a basis for reopening or how it would constitute an exception to the 90-day filing requirement.
 
The respondent has not established that his untimely motion to reopen should be granted, and the motion will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of the motion is moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: HISHAM HADI AL MUGOTIR
File: AXXX XX0 436 - Detroit, MI
September 26, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael B. Dobson
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on December 6, 2011, when we affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge dated August 12, 2010, which denied the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings.1 On January 4, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider our decision. The motion to reconsider will be granted and the appeal is dismissed. The request for refund of the motion fee is denied. The request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
 
We review the findings of fact, including determinations of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether or not the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Iraq, seeks reconsideration by the Board of its December 6, 2011, decision. He alleges we erred in stating he did not submit an appellate brief and, with his motion to reconsider, he has provided a copy of a brief he submitted to the Board concerning his previous appeal. The brief is date stamped indicating that it was received timely by the Board, but it was not associated with the respondent’s record of proceeding. In our previous decision, we stated that no brief had been submitted, but we considered the arguments raised by the respondent previously in his motions to reopen and to reconsider filed with the Immigration Judge (BIA, Dec. 6, 2011, at 2).
 
A motion to reconsider is “a request that the Board reexamine its previous decision in light of additional arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.” Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57 (BIA 2006). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Id. The motion must specify the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision, and it must be supported by pertinent authority. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(c)(6)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). Thus, given that the respondent’s appellate brief was overlooked, we grant his motion to reconsider.
 
In his brief, the respondent argues that the previous order of removal entered in absentia should be rescinded. He argues that he has presented exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear as he was ineffectively served by previous counsel and suffered a panic attack concerning his appearance in Immigration Court. He also argues that he is eligible for cancellation of removal and asylum based on changed country conditions in his native Iraq. After considering the arguments raised in the brief, we will again dismiss the appeal.
 
*2 We have reconsidered our December 6, 2011, decision based on the respondent’s appellate brief. We conclude that he has essentially presented the same arguments raised in his previous motions that we considered at the time of our previous decision. As we found previously, he has failed to support his arguments concerning any ineffective assistance of counsel during the earlier proceedings nor has he complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangley & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). Further, we continue to find that the respondent has failed to show that any anxiety or panic attacks he suffered due to his fear of removal present exceptional circumstances that excuse his failure to appear for his hearing. See Matter of J-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 33 (BIA 1998); see also Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 411 (6th Cir. 2003) (respondent bears burden of proof to show exceptional circumstances). The respondent’s perfunctory statements concerning his panic attacks, with no detail concerning the severity or evidence of treatment, fail to support his burden of showing exceptional circumstances. See Matter of J-P-, supra. As we are dismissing the appeal from the denial of the motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order and the respondent is currently not in the United States, he has not shown any eligibility for relief and the appeal will be dismissed. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Michael J. Creppy
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 5178781 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEISY ALVARENGA-RIVERA
File: AXXX XX4 966 - San Antonio, TX
September 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose M. Guerrero, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 15, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider the denial of her motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by not finding that her failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances. The respondent’s appeal will be sustained and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we disagree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2); 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). According to the evidence in the record, the respondent relied on the advice of her counsel’s office that she did not need to appear for her scheduled hearing. Hence, we find that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel, which prevented the respondent from attending her scheduled hearing before the Immigration Judge.
 
Inasmuch as the respondent has demonstrated that she received ineffective assistance of counsel, we will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with a hearing regarding her removability from the United States and eligibility for relief from removal. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
Sharon Hoffman
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178781 (BIA)

2012 WL 5178779 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHALVA KAHADZE SHALIBASHVILI
File: AXXX XX4 194 - Newark, NJ
September 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Irwin J. Berowitz, Esquire1
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Georgia, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 4, 2012, denying his motion to reopen these removal proceedings which concluded with the respondent being ordered removed in absentia on August 31, 2011. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand.
 
The Department of Homeland Security has informed this Board that, on July 25, 2012, the respondent was removed from the United States. Given that the respondent was not subject to a stay of removal at that time, we conclude that the respondent’s removal from the United States resulted in the withdrawal of his appeal, and the initial decision of the Immigration Judge to deny the respondent’s motion is accordingly final to the same extent as though no appeal had been taken. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.4; cf. section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(b)(5)(C) (providing that the filing of a motion to reopen, in some circumstances, shall stay the removal of the alien “pending disposition of the motion by the [I]mmigration [J]udge”); Matter of Diaz-Garcia, 25 I&N Dec. 794 (BIA 2012). The respondent’s removal also results in a withdrawal of his untimely and number-barred motion to remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d); Desai v. U.S. Att’y Gen., -- F.3d. --, No. 11-3229, 2012 WL 3570718 (3d Cir Aug. 21, 2012); Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).
 
Assuming, arguendo, that the respondent’s removal did not result in a withdrawal of his appeal and his motion to remand, we would affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision and deny the motion. The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely as it was filed on April 17, 2012, approximately 230 days after he was ordered removed. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i), (c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act. The respondent’s motion to remand is also untimely and number-barred. See Matter of Oparah, supra. While the respondent does argue that his prior counsel was ineffective, he does not argue that the applicable time and number limits governing motions to reopen should be tolled on the basis of equitable grounds. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248 (3d Cir. 2005). A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not, by itself, permit an alien to file a motion to reopen at any time and without regard to the number limits. In his untimely and number-barred motion to remand, the respondent does not pursue his claim that he did not receive notice of his removal hearing on August 31, 2011. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii). Instead, he acknowledges that, on June 10, 2011, he appeared before an Immigration Judge and that the Immigration Judge rescheduled these proceedings to August 31, 2011 (Respondent’s Affidavit at ¶ 13). Even if conditions in Georgia have changed since the respondent entered this country in 1999, he does not allege that conditions have materially changed since he was ordered removed in August 2011. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act. We also conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation which would warrant sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Additionally, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated that reopened proceedings are warranted as a matter of discretion. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988). The respondent, by his own admissions, was using controlled substances while his proceedings were pending before the Immigration Judge (Respondent’s Affidavit at ¶ 12). Even though the respondent, an alien who has not complied with the terms of his 1999 admission as a nonimmigrant, has referred to adjustment of status, cancellation of removal, asylum, and withholding of removal, the respondent has not presented persuasive evidence to establish that he is actually eligible for those forms of relief from removal. Considering that the respondent has already been removed from this country and the speculative likelihood that the respondent would be granted relief from removal, we also conclude that reopening is not warranted as a matter of discretion. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal and motion to remand are deemed withdrawn as a result of his removal from the United States and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

Attorney Berowitz’s request to withdraw as the respondent’s attorney of record was denied on September 17, 2012. See Matter of Rosales, 19 I&N Dec. 655 (BIA 1988).

2012 WL 5178741 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR OROBIO A.K.A. EDGAR OROBIO MENA
File: AXXX XX8 607 - Newark, NJ
September 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Ines Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was most recently before us on June 19, 2012, when this Board denied the respondent’s previous untimely motion to reopen his removal proceedings. This motion was filed on July 17, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent characterizes this motion as a motion to reconsider the Board’s previous decision. A motion to reconsider is one which identifies a material error of fact or law in the decision for which reconsideration is being requested. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). The respondent’s motion does not identify any errors of fact or law in the prior decision. Instead, the motion seeks to introduce additional evidence in support of the respondent’s prior motion, which sought reopening based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, this motion is actually in the style of the respondent’s second, untimely motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent’s prior motion was denied because the untimeliness of the motion was not related to the ineffective assistance, because the respondent had not shown due diligence, because the respondent had not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and because the respondent had not included the documents which his prior attorney should ostensibly have submitted at the trial level. This motion is accompanied by the respondent’s affidavit and the evidence which he claims should have been submitted to the Immigration Judge. The affidavit generally states that the respondent “contacted [his] attorney at the time afterwards” and was “assured” that “he would have this matter reopened” (Motion, Tab A). He further states that after his prior attorney was “convicted” the respondent “saw many attorneys who told [him] that there was no possibility of re-opening” the case (Id). The respondent’s affidavit does not provide clear details regarding what transpired between the time of his final hearing on April 1, 2005, and the filing of his first motion with the Board on February 8, 2012.
 
These general, vague allegations do not persuade us that equitable tolling of the time and number limits is warranted. Alzaarir v. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011). As noted in the Board’s prior decision, the respondent was aware at the trial level that his attorney neglected to submit pertinent documents, and the respondent raised allegations of ineffective assistance through new counsel during the course of his appeal. The respondent has not meaningfully explained why he was unable to make a timely motion to reopen, or comply with Lozada’s procedural requirements during the course of his appeal. His claim that he “saw many attorneys” who refused to file a motion is undercut by his ability to gain new counsel for his appeal who filed a motion to remand which alleged ineffective assistance. We again conclude the respondent has not established that the ineffective assistance is tied to the untimeliness or number-barred nature of this motion, and that the respondent has not established due diligence. This motion to reopen will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178741 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 5178747 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SARBPREET SINGH
File: AXXX XX5 561 - Dallas, TX
September 24, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicolas Chavez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on June 18, 2012, when we issued two separate decisions in these proceedings. First, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 22, 2011, decision as untimely and denied his motion to remand. Then, in a separate decision, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 5, 2012, decision denying his motion to reconsider. On July 17, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider and reopen.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. For the reasons set out below, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion is both a motion to reopen and a motion to reconsider. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based upon the previous factual record, whereas a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006). A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra; Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). Here, the respondent seeks reopening based on statements in his brief and previous motions and challenges the decisions of the Board and the Immigration Judge. Consequently, we shall treat the respondent’s motion as both a motion to reconsider and a motion to reopen.
 
We first address that aspect of the motion requesting reconsideration. The respondent’s motion is precluded by regulation, as a party “may not seek reconsideration of a decision denying a previous motion to reconsider.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The motion therefore will be denied as barred by regulation. Additionally, even if we reached the merits of the motion, it essentially reiterates the arguments previously raised in the respondent’s motions and on appeal with regard to his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of O-S-G-, supra (discussing regulatory requirement that a motion to reconsider specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision”); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
As a motion to reopen, it is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Immigration Judge’s final order of removal dated November 22, 2011. Sections 240(c)(7)(C)(i), of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, a motion seeking reopening to reapply for adjustment of status does not fall within any exception to the time limitations on motions to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3) (setting forth exceptions to the time and number limits); see Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009).
 
*2 The respondent continues to assert that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel and thus the time and numerical limitations on his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. Although the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not squarely stated whether an alien has a constitutional right to effective counsel or whether equitable tolling would apply to motions raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, it has nevertheless stated that an alien’s right to due process is violated when the representation he receives was so deficient it impinges on the fairness of the proceedings and the alien, as a result, suffers substantial prejudice. See Guitierrez-Morales v. Homan, 461 F.3d 605, 609 (5th Cir. 2006) (citations omitted).
 
In support of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent again submits the corroboratory evidence from his previous appeal and motions and also submits additional evidence of his compliance with the requirements laid out in Matter of Loazada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 2000). We note that among this evidence is a letter from the Texas Board of Disciplinary Appeals indicating that it dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Office of the Chief Disciplinary Counsel of the State Bar of Texas’s dismissal of his complaint against Mr. Michael Canton (see Motion to Reopen, Tab 4). The Texas’s State Bar determined that the conduct alleged by the respondent was not a violation of the Texas Disciplinary Rules of Professional Conduct (Id). Moreover, the additional evidence submitted by the respondent with this motion was considered by the Immigration Judge in his January 5, 2012, decision, wherein he determined that it would not have changed the outcome of the respondent’s case (see Decision of the Immigration Judge, dated January 5, 2012, at 3). As the respondent has not established prejudice as it relates to his applications for relief, he has not shown that equitable tolling of the time and number limitations is warranted.2
 
Furthermore, the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretionary authority to reopen his proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “the power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). While the respondent continues to argue that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not established prejudice from his prior counsel’s actions or inactions nor has he established prima facie eligibility for any relief given the Immigration Judge’s previous discretionary denial. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*3 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent is not seeking reconsideration of the Board’s decision declining jurisdiction over his untimely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 22, 2011, decision.

2

Regarding equitable tolling in the immigration context, the Fifth Circuit has held that the Board’s exercise of such authority is akin to our exercise of authority in reopening a matter sua sponte, such that the Circuit Court does not have the jurisdiction to review alleged errors in the application of the doctrine. See Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 220 (5th Cir. 2008).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICHOLAS DONALD DALE RUSSELL
File: AXXX XX1 224 - Las Vegas, NV
September 21, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas Jalaie, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board in 2005 when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying a previous motion to reopen these proceedings, in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia in 2001. The respondent has filed another untimely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence that is material to the respondent’s claims. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Because the respondent has not submitted evidence with the motion, it will be denied for not complying with the regulatory requirements for motions to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Even if we considered the merits of the motion, it would be denied, as a motion to reopen is not an opportunity to repeat previously rejected arguments. See INS v. Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991). Nor has the respondent meaningfully raised ineffective assistance of counsel, where he has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), or shown prejudice.
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation warranting the exercise of our discretion to reopen the proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes

2012 WL 5178753 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SASENARINE SINGH
File: AXXX XX9 499 - New York, NY
September 20, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lena Golovnin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This is the respondent’s fifth motion before the Board and it will be denied as untimely and number barred. This case was last before the Board on February 29, 2012, when we denied the respondent’s untimely and number barred motion to reopen these removal proceedings. The final administrative order in these proceedings was entered on July 22, 2003, almost a decade ago. With certain exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order, and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until June 15, 2012, and its supplemental filings on July 9 and September 12, 2012. The motion to reopen and its supplements are filed years out of time and are number-barred. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion.
 
The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at the time his appeal was dismissed by the Board in 2003. He asserts counsel failed to file a timely petition for review of our decision or a timely motion to reopen these proceedings. The respondent therefore claims he is entitled to equitable tolling of the filing deadline for a motion to reopen. However, even if the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel as required in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), he has not demonstrated due diligence in his filing of the instant motion or his prior motions. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008); see also Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 135 (2d Cir. 2000). The respondent has not explained his actions in waiting more than 9 years after the issuance of the final order, or even 3 years after his first untimely motion to reopen was denied by this Board, to file the ineffective assistance claim. Moreover, the respondent has not shown how he was prejudiced by prior counsel’s actions. The respondent has not established that he was not removable as charged, that he would be eligible to pursue adjustment of status in these proceedings, or that he did not have a fair hearing on his application for asylum, withholding of removal or protection under the Convention Against Torture. In short, he has not shown that the outcome of these proceedings would be different but for the actions of prior counsel.
 
*2 The respondent also has not presented any evidence of changed country conditions in order to except him from the time and number restrictions in filing his motion to reopen.
 
Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation that warrants our reopening sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 5178758 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSWAIN BRENTNOL WALCOTT
File: AXXX XX2 779 - Lumpkin, GA
September 19, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board when we returned the record to the Immigration Court, because we found that the respondent waived appeal through counsel. Thus, the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering the respondent removed to Guyana became the final administrative order. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider our decision and reopen proceedings.1 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
A party seeking reconsideration requests that the Board’s original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument that was overlooked. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The respondent has not identified errors of fact or law in our prior decision. Instead, the respondent repeats the arguments he previously made to the Board. Compare Respondent’s Motion with Respondent’s Notice of Appeal, attached statement. We considered these arguments and found them unpersuasive before concluding that the respondent waived appeal through counsel. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (stating that “a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal ....”). As we explained, ineffective assistance is not clear from the record, and the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for raising such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Nor, for reasons set forth, did his filing reflect circumstances that warranted review of the Immigration Judge’s decision by certification. Thus, while the respondent may disagree with our decision, he has presented no arguments that convince us of a factual or legal error, or an appellate argument that we overlooked.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen will also be denied. Notwithstanding the respondent’s recent partial compliance with Matter of Lozada - filing a complaint with prior counsel’s state bar - the respondent has still not shown that counsel’s alleged deficient performance resulted in prejudice. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005) (concluding that an alien must show prejudice when he advances an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and that “[p]rejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different”). Neither the respondent’s present or previous filing included proffered evidence reflecting prima facie evidence of United States citizenship. Additionally, the respondent has presented no evidence that his criminal conviction underlying these proceedings has been overturned or that a state court has otherwise found ineffective assistance by the respondent’s defense counsel of the type in Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010). Finally, the respondent has not alleged or demonstrated prima facie eligibility for relief from removal.
 
*2 The respondent has submitted a photocopy of a portion of a detention center computer-based mailing log and submits that his notice of appeal would have been timely but for unspecified administrative errors within the detention center’s mailing system. However, even if the respondent had timely filed the Notice of Appeal, it would not impact our conclusion that he waived appeal through counsel. A respondent who waives appeal cannot thereafter file an appeal. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) (final sentence), 1003.39.
 
Finally, we do not find an adequate basis for reconsidering our previous decision or reopening the case sua sponte. Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

We will treat the motion to reconsider as timely.
