2012 WL 5178738 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PHILIP MENSAH ANKUH
File: AXXX XX4 256 - New York, NY
September 17, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Olu Jaiyebo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 1, 2011, denying his motion to reopen. An Immigration Judge previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on February 8, 2011. On appeal, the respondent maintains that he failed to appear due to exceptional circumstances, and he asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopen proceedings sua sponte. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that reopening is warranted because he failed to appear for his hearing due to exceptional circumstances. See section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). Upon de novo review, we find that the respondent’s contentions that he misunderstood his attorney’s directions regarding his obligation to appear at the hearing do not constitute exceptional circumstances within the meaning of section 240(e)(1) of the Act, when the respondent has not set forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. To the extent the respondent contends that he did not appear because he was relying on a pending motion to continue that was not received by the Immigration Court until the day after his hearing, we note that parties are obligated to appear for a scheduled hearing unless and until the Immigration Judge expressly grants a request for a continuance. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (the filing of a motion for a continuance does not excuse an alien from failing to appear); Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988).
 
Furthermore, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established that sua sponte reopening of his proceedings is warranted. The respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation so as to justify a discretionary grant of his untimely motion to reopen and the extraordinary remedy of reopening his removal proceedings sua sponte. See, e.g., Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (discretion to reopen a case sua sponte is “an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). While we recognize that the respondent is married to a United States citizen, the two visa petitions she has filed on his behalf have been denied. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178738 (BIA)

2012 WL 5178735 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIELA CORDOVA-SOTO A.K.A. GABRIELA CORDOVA
File: AXXX XX5 891 - Kansas City, MO
September 17, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claudia Valenzuela, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Melissa L. Castillo
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, who is a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed a June 6, 2012, decision, in which an Immigration Judge denied her motion to reopen. Both the respondent and the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), as well as amicus curiae, have filed briefs concerning the appeal, which will be dismissed.1
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i),(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent adjusted to lawful permanent resident status on July 3, 1991. She was convicted in Kansas on May 3, 2002, of theft, and on March 20, 2003, of giving a worthless check. She was convicted on October 21, 2005, of possession of methamphetamine, a felony under Kansas law.
 
On October 21, 2005, she was charged with being subject to removal under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), as having been convicted of an aggravated felony relating to trafficking in a controlled substance; under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act, for having been convicted of two crimes of moral turpitude not arising out of a single scheme of misconduct, and under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, for having been convicted of a violation relating to a controlled substance.
 
The respondent on November 1, 2005, signed a “Stipulated Request For Issuance of Final Order Of Removal, Waiver of Appearance and Hearing.” Consequently, on November 8, 2005, an Immigration Judge issued a decision finding that the respondent had waived a hearing before an Immigration Judge, admitted the truthfulness of the allegations and the charges in the Notice to Appear, conceded that she was ineligible for relief from removal, and requested a removal order. The Immigration Judge thus ordered the respondent removed to Mexico.
 
The respondent was removed to Mexico on November 10, 2005, and returned to the United States on November 27, 2005. A DHS official reinstated the previous removal order under section 241(a)(5) of the Act in Kansas on September 23, 2010, and she was again removed to Mexico, where she remains.
 
On October 17, 2011, the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit dismissed the respondent’s petition for review concerning the September 23, 2010, reinstatement order. Cordova-Soto v. Holder, 659 F.3d 1029 (10th Cir. 2011).2
 
*2 The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court on January 24, 2012, concerning the November 8, 2005, removal order. She argued that the 2005 removal order should be reopened and rescinded, as the stipulation she signed was not knowing and intelligent. That is, according to the respondent, she was advised that she was not eligible for relief from removal, when later, in 2006, persons in her situation became eligible to seek cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. Gonzales-Gomez v. Achim, 441 F.3d 532 (7th Cir. 2006)(drug offense that was classified as a felony under state law, but only a misdemeanor under the Controlled Substances Act, did not constitute an aggravated felony under the Immigration and Nationality Act).
 
The Immigration Judge held on June 6, 2012, that there were “multiple grounds” for denying the motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge correctly found that the motion was untimely filed, I.J. at 3, as the motion was filed more than six years after the final administrative order of November 8, 2005. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1)(motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the entry of a final order of removal); DHS Br. at 4-5.3 The motion to reopen fit no exception to untimely motions under section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act. The arguments against the stipulated order of removal belatedly raised by the respondent, Respondent’s Br. at 18, and amicus, could have been presented in a timely motion to reopen.
 
Moreover, there was no basis for “equitable tolling” of the motions deadline, compare Respondent’s Br. at 18-20, where there was no claim of ineffective assistance of counsel presented, or other reason to show that the respondent was unaware of the status of her case, which could possibly excuse the lateness of the motion. El-Gazawy v. Holder, ___ F.3d ___, 2012 WL 3517596 (7th Cir. 2012)(“equitable tolling requires a court to consider whether a reasonable person in the claimant’s position would have been aware of the possibility that he had suffered an injury”)(citation omitted).
 
The Immigration Judge also properly concluded that the respondent failed to establish exceptional circumstances which would warrant sua sponte reopening under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). I.J. at 3-4; Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent’s appeal will, therefore, be dismissed.4
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 5178824 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DIALLO LASSINA
File: AXXX XX6 786 - Newark, NJ
September 12, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Terry Smart, Jr., Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 9, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that he has demonstrated exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear, due to ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent’s appeal will be sustained.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we find it appropriate to reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for a new hearing. The record reflects that the Immigration Judge conducted a full evidentiary hearing on March 27, 2000. At the conclusion of the hearing, the Immigration Judge provided the respondent with 30 days to submit additional documentation in support of his applications for relief from removal and stated that he would set a new court date to go over the additional evidence (Tr. at 101). On April 28, 2000, the Immigration Judge held a hearing; however, neither the respondent nor his counsel were present (Tr. at 103). The respondent also did not submit any additional evidence within the 30 day period allowed by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 103).
 
It is unclear from the record whether the respondent or his counsel of record was informed of the date and time of the April 28, 2000, hearing. The record contains a notice of hearing dated March 27, 2000, providing that the respondent was scheduled for a hearing on April 28, 2000, at 9 a.m. However, the certificate of service is blank and the transcript of the respondent’s March 27, 2000, hearing does not indicate that the Immigration Judge necessarily intended to hold a subsequent hearing, if the respondent did not submit additional evidence within the 30 day period. Even if the Immigration Judge intended to hold a hearing on April 28, 2000, he did not orally advise the respondent or his counsel of the time of the hearing. Under these circumstances, we will reopen the respondent’s removal proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with an opportunity to appear for a hearing. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained, removal proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
*2 Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
Elise L. Manuel
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178824 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KAZIMIERZ TOMALA
File: AXXX XX4 765 - Chicago, IL
September 10, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sara Bartos, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mirta J. Woodall
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 An Immigration Judge has certified the case to the Board under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). The record will again be remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
The respondent, who is a native and citizen of Poland, was ordered removed in absentia on February 18, 2004. An Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen on August 24, 2011. The Immigration Judge concluded that the removal hearing notice was sent to the respondent’s attorney of record, Robert E. Coughlon, Jr., and the respondent did not present a sufficient claim concerning any ineffective assistance of counsel provided by Coughlon.
 
The respondent filed a timely appeal with the Board concerning the Immigration Judge’s August 24, 2011, decision. The Board issued a decision on December 9, 2011. We set out the respondent’s claim on appeal that he did not receive proper notice of his removal hearing, inasmuch as it was mailed to Coughlon, whom the respondent maintained he did not retain to represent him in removal proceedings. We went on to remand the case to the Immigration Court, as the record was incomplete. That is, we stated in the December 9, 2011, decision:
According to the Immigration Judge’s decision, the record contains a Notice of Entry of Appearance before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) on behalf of the respondent by attorney Robert Coughlon, Jr. (I.J. at 2). However, a review of the record prepared for this Board does not contain this document. Given the respondent’s arguments regarding his representation before the Immigration Court by Robert Coughlon, Jr., we find it necessary to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to ensure that the Board has a complete record of the respondent’s proceedings before the Immigration Court.
 
 
According to the record, an Immigration Judge scheduled a master calendar hearing concerning the respondent for June 6, 2012. It appears that a very short hearing was held that day, which related to the respondent and other individuals.
 
The Immigration Judge certified the record back to the Board on June 8, 2012. He attached a summary order dated June 6, 2012. The summary order says that the case was remanded by the Board to “reconsider motion to reopen”, which was not the directive in the Board’s December 9, 2011, decision. The Immigration Judge does not address the actual substance of the Board’s December 9, 2011, remand order, which in fact concerned an incomplete record. We therefore will again remand the record to the Immigration Court, so that the Immigration Judge may consider, and address, the fact that the record is incomplete.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings not inconsistent with the foregoing opinion and our decision of December 9, 2011.

2012 WL 5178809 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: STEPHEN GIKERA NJOGU
File: AXXX XX6 041 - Dallas, TX
September 6, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
N. Jude Menes, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cynthia D. Goodman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 22, 2010 decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent filed his motion with the Immigration Judge on December 2, 2010 while claiming that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at the time of the Immigration Judge’s August 5, 2010 pre-conclusion voluntary departure grant under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We hold that the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. In particular, while the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge improperly focused on the untimely nature of his motion in light of his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance (i.e., which may toll the filing deadline for his motion), we find that his claim for reopening should be denied as he failed to show any prejudice given that he is not currently eligible for any form of relief (Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) Br. at 1). See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988) (an applicant seeking reopening must establish prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought).
 
The respondent maintains that his former counsel failed to appear in court for his master calendar hearing--which allegedly led to the respondent accepting voluntary departure despite lacking full understanding of its implications--and that such counsel falsely promised various forms of immigration relief, actions which ultimately deprived the respondent of “an opportunity to present the bona fides of his valid and legitimate marriage to a U.S. citizen” (Respondent’s Br. at 4-6, 10-11). Even accepting the respondent’s contention that he substantively complied with the procedural requirements described in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not established prejudice from the actions of his prior counsel as he has not provided any evidence showing that the outcome of his case might have been different if such counsel acted with sufficient competence. See U.S. v. Herrera, 412 F.3d 577, 580 (5th Cir. 2005) (noting that in order to prove prejudice, an applicant must show that there is a reasonable probability that, but for counsel’s unprofessional errors, the result of the proceeding would have been different) (internal citations omitted). In particular, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that had his counsel been effective, the respondent would have presented evidence demonstrating that his marriage was legitimate as he has not addressed, much less reconciled, the fact that the DHS previously issued an intent to deny his wife’s petition for alien relative (Form I-130) that was filed on his behalf upon concluding that their marriage was not bona fide and that it was entered into for the purpose of evading immigration laws (Exh. 2; Respondent’s Br. at 8, 11). See, e.g., Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009); Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). While the respondent further states that he and his wife remain married and that another I-130 petition that was filed on his behalf is pending with the DHS, we are not inclined to remand the matter for additional proceedings to await adjudication of such a petition based on the particular facts of this case (Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). See generally Morgan v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 549, 552 (2d Cir. 2006) (finding no abuse of discretion in the denial of a continuance for adjudication of a second visa petition when the first I-130 filed by the same petitioner was denied by the USCIS for failure to establish a bona fide marriage).
 
*2 To the extent the respondent contends that his former counsel misrepresented his eligibility for asylum, the respondent has not presented any intelligible argument suggesting that he may be able to demonstrate eligibility for asylum or any other form of relief (Respondent’s Br. at 8). See, e.g., Matter of Almanza, 24 I&N Dec. 771 (BIA 2009) (the burden is on the respondent to establish eligibility for relief). As we see no evidence before us indicating that a remand of the proceedings might lead to a different result in this matter, the respondent’s charges of ineffective assistance of counsel do not undermine the Immigration Judge’s finding of removability or underlying decision.
 
Further, while the respondent has filed supplemental evidence on appeal, we may not accept these documents into the record as we are precluded from engaging in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003-1(d)(3)(iv). Even considering such information, the respondent has not identified any specific evidence which, if presented, would have affected the proceeding below, nor has he illustrated how the Immigration Judge’s decision remains incomplete or defective. As such, the respondent has not established that a remand is warranted for any reason. See Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031 (BIA 1999). We also find that the circumstances presented by the respondent are not “exceptional” as to justify a sua sponte reopening of the matter (Respondent’s Br. at 11-12). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). To the extent that the respondent seeks a stay of removal, he has not shown why the execution of his removal order should be stayed and we accordingly deny his request in this regard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f). Accordingly, based on the foregoing, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 5178809 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIANLING LIAN
File: AXXX XX7 991 - Baltimore, MD
August 30, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
K. Thomas Li, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of China, was ordered removed in absentia on March 8, 2004. On January 15, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on June 26, 2009. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that his motion was untimely and did not warrant sua sponte reopening. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied.
 
Upon review, we find that in light of the circumstances presented in this case, including evidence reflecting that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance with respect to the respondent’s application for adjustment of status which resulted in the issuance of the Notice to Appear and the respondent being placed into proceedings as well as his direction to the respondent not to appear at his March 8, 2004, hearing, we will allow the respondent another opportunity to appear for a hearing. We note that the respondent acted diligently upon discovering his former counsel’s deficient performance. In reaching this conclusion, we further note that he respondent is the beneficiary of an approved I-130.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Sharon C Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 3911858 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LIANG QING JIANG
File: AXXX XX5 249 - New York, NY
August 29, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Morris M. Wu, Esquire
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of China. He has appealed from the decision of an Immigration Judge, mailed on June 11, 2012, denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings, conducted in absentia on February 14, 2000. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not filed a brief concerning the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i),(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent argued in his motion to reopen that an “exceptional circumstance” prevented his appearance at his removal hearing. That is, he claimed that counsel gave him an incorrect removal hearing date of February 15, 2000, and he learned on that date that he had been ordered removed in absentia the day before.
 
Even assuming that the respondent’s attorney provided an incorrect hearing date, and that such advice could be considered an “exceptional circumstance” for failing to appear, the respondent did not file his motion to reopen within the 180 day period provided for seeking reopening of an in absentia order based on “exceptional circumstances.” Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. §1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); I.J. at 2.1
 
Nor did the respondent demonstrate that he acted with due diligence in seeking reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. I.J. at 2. According to the respondent’s affidavit, he was aware since February 15, 2000, that he had been ordered removed in absentia, but filed the motion to reopen over 12 years later.
 
We find, as did the Immigration Judge, that the respondent did not pursue reopening of these proceedings with due diligence. Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 131-32 (2d Cir. 2008); Chen v. Gonzales, 437 F.3d 267, 268 (2d Cir. 2006); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir. 2006); I.J. at 2. Under these circumstances, the 180-day deadline for seek reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was not tolled. The motion to reopen was untimely filed. The Immigration Judge’s decision will, therefore, be affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent argues on appeal that he did not receive notice of the removal hearing. Rather, the respondent received proper notice of the hearing from the Immigration Court, as written notice of the hearing was provided to his counsel of record on September 23, 1999. Notice to counsel constitutes notice to a respondent. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). To the extent that the respondent blamed counsel for his failure to appear at the removal hearing, his claim was that he failed to appear due to exceptional circumstances. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; I.J. at 2; Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996).

2012 WL 3911861 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARBEL BALMORE MORENO
File: AXXX XX0 769 - Harlingen, TX
August 28, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Liliana L. Quiroz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard I. Newman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a male native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision mailed on March 19, 2012, that denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. The respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear (NTA) on July 7, 2003, listing the time and place of his hearing and the consequences of any failure to appear (Exh. 1). We therefore agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent received proper notice pursuant to our decision in Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 185 (BIA 2001) (stating that the entry of an in absentia order is authorized when the alien has been given written notice of the removal hearing). Further, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments on appeal that he did not receive proper notice because he was a minor. The Form I-213 reflects that the respondent was 19 years old when the NTA was personally served (Exh. 2). Accordingly, additional notice provisions do not apply in this case. See Lopez-Dubon v. Holder, 609 F.3d 642 (5th Cir. 2010) (concluding that notice must be served on an adult only for aliens under 14 years of age); Matter of Cubor, 25 I&N Dec. 470 (BIA 2011) (holding that personal service of a charging document on a minor who is 14 years of age or older is effective service).
 
The respondent also argues that he failed to appear due to exceptional circumstances. In particular, the respondent claims that he hired a purported attorney to file a change of venue motion on his behalf and that this individual advised the respondent not to appear for his hearing in Harlingen, Texas. However, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is untimely as it was not filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see also Vaz Dos Reis v. Holder, 606 F.3d 1, 5 (1st Cir. 2010) (stating that with respect to motions to reopen in absentia proceedings courts typically treat ineffective assistance of counsel claims as an exceptional circumstance). To the extent that the respondent requests equitable tolling of the riling deadline, we note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not held in a published decision that filing deadlines before this Board may be equitably tolled. Rather, in Ramos-Bonilla v. Mukasey, 543 F.3d 216, 220 (5th Cir. 2008), the court found that a request for equitable tolling was in effect a request that the Board exercise sua sponte authority over an untimely motion under at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and that the court lacks jurisdiction to review a decision by the Board not to exercise this authority.
 
*2 We conclude that the respondent has not established an exceptional circumstance herein to warrant the sua sponte reopening of these proceedings. The respondent was personally served with a NTA listing the time and place of his hearing. Moreover, the record reflects that he was provided oral notice in the Spanish language of his hearing date and the consequences of failing to appear (Exh. 1). He has also not established what efforts he took to determine the status of his removal proceeding after receiving the NTA more than 8 years before he filed his motion to reopen. Cf. Rodriguez-Manzano v. Holder, 666 F.3d 948, 954 (5th Cir. 2012) (finding with respect to cases that commenced before 1992 that a motion to reopen could not be denied based on lack of due diligence). We decline to reopen proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911861 (BIA)

2012 WL 3911836 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERARDO CAMPOS-MORALES
File: AXXX XX8 012 - Seattle, WA
August 27, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David N. Shomloo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Love
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On July 19, 2012, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen in proceedings in which the Board dismissed his appeal on December 22, 2011. The Board granted his request for a stay of removal on July 24, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); see also Azarte v. Ashcroft, 394 F.3d 1278, 1283-84 (9th Cir. 2005). However, the respondent asks that the Board reissue our prior decision as he contends that he was afforded ineffective assistance by former counsel, Ms. Stevens (Motion at 4). Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). Specifically, he contends Ms. Stevens did not inform him of the Board’s adverse decision, which prevented him from filing a timely petition for review with the circuit court (Motion at 6, 10). He also asks that the Board grant his motion sua sponte (Motion at 7, 16).
 
Although in rare cases we have reissued decisions in order to remedy a lack of notice arising from the ineffective assistance of an alien’s counsel of record, this is reserved for very exceptional circumstances not present here. The respondent has submitted evidence reflecting that he informed Ms. Stevens of the allegations against her, and that he filed a bar complaint in this regard. However, he has not demonstrated that he experienced prejudice as a result of Ms. Stevens’ actions, as is required for reopening pursuant to a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iturribarria v. INS, supra.
 
In his declaration in support of his motion the respondent asks that the Board reissue its decision, stating that he is “eligible for cancellation of removal” (Decl. at 3). However, as the DHS notes, the respondent is ineligible for such relief as his period of continuous physical presence was terminated on June 26, 2008, when he was voluntarily returned to his native Mexico (Exh. 2). The respondent also has not submitted an application for cancellation with his motion, as required. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). As the respondent has not demonstrated any plausible ground for relief, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by Ms. Stevens’ actions. Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir. 2003); see also INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). Moreover, we do not find that this case presents exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the following order shall be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911836 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 3911845 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOUN LUNG ZHANG
File: AXXX XX0 747 - New York, New York
August 24, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fuhao Yang, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a 30-year-old male native and citizen of China, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s written decision dated April 28, 2011, which denied his motion to rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review for clear error the findings of fact, including the credibility determination, made by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review de novo all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993).
 
The respondent was issued a Notice to Appear on October 29, 2002 (Exh. 1). Through his prior attorney, Liu Yu, he requested a change of venue from San Antonio, Texas, to New York City, which was granted on December 27, 2002. He appeared on January 23, 2003, in New York City with counsel, and he was issued a hearing notice for July 3, 2003 (Exh. 3). He retained Charles Day as his attorney on January 23, 2003.
 
Following a hearing conducted in absentia on July 3, 2003, at which the respondent failed to appear, an Immigration Judge found him subject to removal as charged, determined that he had abandoned all potential applications for relief, and ordered him removed from the United States. See generally section 240 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a.
 
In his motion to reopen filed on February 25, 2011, the respondent alleged that when he arrived for his hearing on July 3, 2003, Mr. Day told him that he was not required to appear that day, that he had requested a continuance and that he would notify him of his next hearing date. He indicated that Mr. Day did not speak Chinese, that he usually communicated with Mr. Day’s assistant who spoke Chinese, and that the assistant was present on July 3, 2003, when he arrived for the hearing. At the beginning of 2005, the respondent indicated that he discovered that Mr. Day’s law office had been closed, he attempted to retain other attorneys who declined to represent him, and he did not discover that he had been ordered removed in absentia until he obtained his alien registration number from his prior attorney, Mr. Yu, in 2006.1 In 2010, he claimed to have learned for the first time that Mr. Day had been disbarred in 2006, he alleges that he filed a bar complaint against him in 2011, and he attached a copy of a certified mail receipt addressed to the New York State Bar disciplinary committee dated February 14, 2011, to his motion. However, he failed to include a copy of the complaint itself. The respondent requested equitable tolling based on the alleged ineffective assistance of Mr. Day.2
 
*2 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) filed a response in opposition to the motion, alleging that the respondent had not established due diligence to qualify for equitable tolling in light of his claim that he discovered the existence of his in absentia removal order in 2006, yet failed to file a motion to reopen until 5 years later without an adequate explanation for the delay.
 
On April 28, 2001, the Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely. She concluded that the respondent had failed to establish that he exercised due diligence to qualify for equitable tolling of the filing deadline in view of his waiting 5 years, until 2011, to file a motion after discovering in 2006 that he had been ordered removed in absentia. She also noted that the respondent did not attach an asylum application to the motion, and that no application ever was filed before the entry of the in absentia removal order.
 
On appeal, the respondent continues to allege that he has qualified for equitable tolling. He also claims that the Immigration Judge was biased against him and failed to address his claim that he attempted to exercise due diligence, but that the attorneys he contacted allegedly declined to represent him after they learned that his case had been assigned to her.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent received personal service of his hearing for July 3, 2003. Although he learned that he had been ordered removed in absentia in 2006, he failed to file a motion to reopen until 5 years later, in 2011. To the extent that the respondent claims that he was unable to retain another attorney until 2011, he does not explain why he did not contact the Immigration Court directly to resolve his immigration status, or why he did not rehire his prior immigration attorney, Mr. Yu, when he contacted him in 2006 to obtain his alien registration number.
 
Therefore, even if the respondent established ineffective assistance on the part of Mr. Day, he has not demonstrated due diligence to obtain equitable tolling of the deadline to file a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008) (to equitably toll filing deadline for motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, alien must demonstrate that he has exercised due diligence during entire period he seeks to toll, including both before and after ineffective assistance was or should have been discovered, and period from that point until motion is filed); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006) (aliens who waited 2 years to file motion based on ineffective assistance did not establish due diligence to obtain equitable tolling); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000) (alien who waited 2 years to file motion based on ineffective assistance did not establish due diligence).
 
*3 Although the respondent indicates on appeal that he is requesting reopening in order to apply for asylum, he has not established his prima facie eligibility because he has failed to attach a completed asylum application. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In his affidavit attached to his motion, the respondent claimed that other attorneys declined to represent him because of the identity of the Immigration Judge assigned to his case. These claims are wholly unsubstantiated.

2

The Board expelled Mr. Day from practice before the Board, the Immigration Courts and the Department of Homeland Security on January 18, 2007, based on his disbarment in New York on May 4, 2006.

2012 WL 3911846 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR ISRAEL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX3 185 - Los Angeles, CA
August 24, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On August 21, 2009, the respondent failed to appear for his removal hearing and was ordered removed in absentia by the Immigration Judge. The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied in a November 3, 2010, decision. The respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial, but the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal in a June 28, 2011, decision. On July 26, 2012, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board based on the alleged ineffectiveness of his former counsel. For the reasons set out below, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on June 28, 2011. Generally, a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, and only one may be filed. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i)ofthe Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). A request for rescission of an in absentia order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date that the order of removal is issued. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) and 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The present motion was filed on July 26, 2012, well beyond the filing deadlines. In addition, the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen that was denied by the Immigration Judge on November 3, 2010.
 
However, aliens raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in a motion can have the time limits on motions equitably tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Aliens relying on equitable tolling have the burden of demonstrating that they exercised due diligence in discovering the fraud or error.
 
We find that the respondent’s untimely and number-barred motion to reopen does not qualify for equitable tolling. The respondent has not demonstrated that he exercised the requisite diligence in filing the instant motion. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on August 21, 2009, and on June 28, 2011, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2010, decision denying his motion to reopen. However, the respondent waited until July 26, 2012, to file the instant motion, and has not specified what he did between June 28 2011, and July 26, 2012, to protect his rights or when he definitely learned of his prior counsel’s incompetence. Given the lack of evidence regarding the steps he took to preserve his rights from June 2011 to July 2012, we find that the respondent did not act with due diligence. See Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090, 1096 (9th Cir. 2007) (refusing to toll where immigrant failed to investigate suspicions of incompetence for 6 months). In any event, the respondent has not submitted the requisite application for relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1).
 
*2 We alternatively find that the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).1 See generally, Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008). The respondent has not included a personal affidavit or any correspondence to his prior counsel regarding his allegations of ineffectiveness. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004) (finding that alien did not comply with Lozada where he did not submit an affidavit and did not provide former counsel with an adequate opportunity to respond to allegation). The respondent also has not filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority nor adequately explained why he has not done so.
 
Finally, the respondent has not shown that an “exceptional situation” exists that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for a stay is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must satisfy the following procedural requirements: (1) an affidavit of the alien attesting to the relevant facts, including a detailed description of the agreement with former counsel, (2) former counsel must be informed of the allegations and provided with an opportunity to respond, and (3) indicate whether a complaint has been filed the appropriate disciplinary authority, and if not, why not. Lozada, supra.

2012 WL 3911799 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RONNIE VIJAI MOONA A.K.A. RONNIE VIJAL MOONA A.K.A. RONNIE MOONA
File: AXXX XX4 810 - New York, NY
August 24, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richa Puri, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 4, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On appeal, the respondent requests remand on the grounds that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. _, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a motion to vacate it. The respondent has offered evidence of that with the appeal. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Removability and relief from removal are not at issue on appeal.

2

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel. In his brief on appeal, the respondent acknowledges that his motion regarding one of his convictions has been denied by the State Court, but urges that the other is still pending and would affect his eligibility for relief.

3

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

2012 WL 3911848 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANASTACIO ORTIZ-ALVARADO A.K.A. ANASATACIO ORTIZ-ALVARADO
File: AXXX XX3 157 - Lumpkin, GA
August 24, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patrick Anthony Powell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anthony M. Cacavio
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 17, 2012, ordering his removal from the United States.1 We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision in this matter. In this case, the respondent, through counsel, has conceded that he is subject to removal from the United States as a result of his 2010 Georgia state conviction for Possession of Marijuana with Intent to Distribute (I.J. at 1 -2; Tr. at 200; Exh. 1). See section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii). While we recognize that the respondent believes that his criminal defense attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel, it is well settled that the Immigration Judges and this Board cannot entertain collateral attacks upon State court judgments. See Mohammed v. Ashcroft, 261 F.3d 1244, 1251 (11th Cir. 2001); Zinnanti v. INS, 651 F.2d 420, 421 (5th Cir. July 24, 1981);2 Matter of Cuellar, 25 I&N Dec. 850, 854-855 (BIA 2012). If the respondent believes his conviction is unconstitutional, then his remedy is to seek post-conviction relief in the Georgia courts. However, the conviction remains effective for immigration purposes unless and until it is vacated by a court of competent jurisdiction.
 
On appeal, the respondent does not allege that, in light of his conviction he is eligible for any form of relief from removal. Moreover, he does not meaningfully allege on appeal that his desire to pursue post-conviction relief warranted a further continuance of these removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § § 1003.29, 1240.6; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1997); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). Even if such claim was properly before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that, in light of the speculative nature of post-conviction claims, the respondent’s desire to pursue such claims did not warrant a further continuance (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 219-220).
 
As the respondent is subject to removal from the United States as a result of his criminal history and has not established eligibility for relief from removal, the Immigration Judge properly concluded these proceedings by entering an order of removal. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We recognize that the respondent refers to his current detention in his appeal brief. However, removal proceedings are separate and apart from bond proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d). Thus, we lack jurisdiction to consider issues regarding the respondent’s detention in the instant removal proceedings.

2

In Bonner v. City of Prichard, 661 F.2d 1206, 1209 (11th Cir. 1981) (en banc), the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit adopted as binding precedent all Fifth Circuit decisions handed down prior to October 1, 1981.

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MING LING CHEN
File: AXXX XX2 691 - New York, NY
August 22, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerald Karikari, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of the People’s Republic of China, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on September 1, 1999, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on January 25, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied it on March 22, 2011, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent does not argue with particularity on appeal that he did not receive adequate notice of the hearing or that ineffective assistance of counsel warrants reopening based on “exceptional circumstances.” We therefore affirm those aspects of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying reopening to allow him an opportunity to seek adjudication of his application for adjustment of status. The respondent is an arriving alien in removal proceedings.
 
The respondent’s motion to the extent that it seeks adjustment of status consideration was filed more than 90 days after he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. His request for reopening to that extent is therefore untimely. See Matter of Monges, 25 I&N Dec. 246 (BIA 2010); Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998); I.J. at 6. We will additionally not reopen these proceedings sua sponte solely for the purpose of delaying the enforcement of the respondent’s removal order while his adjustment of status application remains pending with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009). The Immigration Judge’s decision is also affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911832 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 3911746 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REY GONZALEZ-MONGE
File: AXXX XX6 541 - Los Angeles, CA
August 16, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision mailed on May 22, 2012, that denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on November 21, 2005. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. There is no issue regarding whether the respondent knew that he had a scheduled removal hearing. Thus, the respondent had notice of his hearing and can seek reopening of proceedings solely upon the basis of exceptional circumstances. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In this case, those circumstances allegedly arise due to the respondent’s claim that on the day of his hearing he was hospitalized due to a serious injury. However, motions to reopen to rescind an in absentia order based upon exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the date of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order. Id. The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed over 6 years after he was ordered removed. His motion to reopen, therefore, was untimely filed, as properly found by the Immigration Judge.
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that even if equitable tolling is taken into consideration, the respondent has not established how his former attorney, Terrence McGuire, prevented him from filing a motion to reopen for over 6 years. In particular, the respondent claims that in November 2005 he hired Mr. McGuire to file a motion to reopen on his behalf. The respondent, however, does not recount when he learned that Mr. McGuire failed to file a motion to reopen in the respondent’s case. To the extent that the respondent indicates for the first time on appeal that he became suspicious of Mr. McGuire in early 2011 after discovering that his phone was disconnected, the respondent has not described in detail why he waited until March 2012 to file a motion to reopen if he first became suspicious in early 2011 of Mr. McGuire. In addition, the respondent has not reported what efforts he took to determine the status of his removal proceedings following his consultation with Mr. McGuire. Consequently, the respondent has not established the requisite due diligence in filing his motion to reopen based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011) (setting forth the requirements for due diligence).
 
*2 Attached to the Notice of Appeal the respondent submitted documentation apparently relating to his exceptional circumstances claim that he suffered a serious injury prior to his November 21, 2005, hearing. We do not consider evidence first submitted on appeal; rather, we review the record before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 & n.10 (BIA 1984); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988). Even if we were to construe the respondent’s additional evidence as a motion to remand, the respondent has not shown that this evidence is new and previously unavailable, or that it could possibly alter the decision in this case. Therefore, we find that a remand is not warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911746 (BIA)
End of Document

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shan D. Potts, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On March 22, 2012, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to terminate these removal proceedings. On May 9, 2012, the respondent filed the instant timely motion to reopen to enable him to apply for adjustment of status based on an approved immediate relative visa petition, dated October 30, 2006, classifying him as the parent of a United States citizen. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a statement opposing this motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent, who was previously represented by the above-named attorney before the Immigration Judge and the Board, now claims that he did not pursue an application for adjustment of status previously in these proceedings due to ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that ineffective assistance of counsel in handling the respondent’s potential eligibility for adjustment is sufficiently clear on the face of the record that reopening is warranted even without full compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Rodriguez-Lariz v. I.N.S., 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002). Accordingly, the respondent’s timely, unopposed motion to reopen will be granted, and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 3911787 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICHAEL BACANI
File: AXXX XX7 800 - Atlanta, GA
August 14, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia A. McKenzie, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mary C. Lee
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 1, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.1 On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
At the hearing, the respondent asked for a continuance to allow him to collaterally attack his conviction (Exh. 3). The Immigration Judge denied the request (I.J. at 3; Tr. at 14-15). The respondent requested no other relief (Tr. at 16).
 
On appeal, the respondent says that the conviction, which serves as the basis for removal, and which renders him ineligible for relief from removal, is being challenged before the criminal court pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. _, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010).2 The respondent alleges that he was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and he has filed a writ of habeas corpus to vacate it. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).3 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue post-conviction relief, a writ of habeas corpus does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The writ does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger

2

2012 WL 3911658 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GIOVANNI JOSE MARTINO MORENO A.K.A. GIOVANNI MARTINO A.K.A. GIOVANNI J. MARTINO A.K.A. JOHNATHON MARTINO A.K.A. GIOVANNI J. GHOST A.K.A. GIOVANNI MARTIN A.K.A. BLOODY G. MARTINO A.K.A. MARTINO GIOVANNI
File: AXXX XX6 466 - New York, NY
August 8, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order of removal in the respondent’s case on July 28, 2011, and on June 27, 2012, the respondent filed the instant motion with the Board. Whether construed as a motion to reopen or as a motion to reconsider, the respondent’s motion is untimely. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991) (discussing motions to reopen and motions to reconsider); sections 240(c)(6)(B), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(B), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b)(2), (c)(2). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in the instant motion. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board). He specifically claims that his former attorney did not properly contest on appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision finding him removable as charged and ineligible for relief; did not file a timely motion to reopen the proceedings; and did not file a timely petition for judicial review of the Board’s prior order.
 
The filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Jin Bo Zhao v. I.N.S., 452 F.3d 154, 160 (2nd Cir. 2006). In order to prevail on his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, however, the respondent must substantially comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2nd Cir. 2005). (“[A]n alien who has failed to comply substantially with the Lozada requirements in her motion to reopen before the BIA forfeits her ineffective assistance of counsel claim”).
 
Before allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. In the present case, the respondent has failed to comply with Lozada’s notice requirement. There is no evidence now before the Board to show that the respondent has made any attempt to inform his former attorney of the allegations against her, or that his former attorney has otherwise been made aware of the allegations. Further, the respondent has not explained why he has not properly notified his former attorney of the allegations against her. Id.
 
*2 In certain circumstances, “slavish adherence” to the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, is unnecessary. See Ruiz-Martinez v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 102, 121 (2d Cir.2008). In the respondent’s case, however, ineffective assistance of counsel is not apparent on the face of the record. See YiLong Yang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133, 143 (2nd Cir.2007). Under the circumstances, we find that the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, supra.
 
Inasmuch as the respondent has not set forth a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim, equitable tolling of the filing deadlines is not warranted in this case. Further, no exception to the filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen appears to apply to this motion, and no exceptional situation has been shown to be present in this case to warrant the exercise of the Board’s limited authority to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(a), (c)(3). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen and reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and reconsider is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 3911658 (BIA)
End of Document

2012 WL 3911707 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHILEREOBI OBIOMA UKATTAH
File: AXXX XX3 371 - San Francisco, CA
August 2, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas M. Lehrman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Gambill
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Nigeria. On November 8, 2010, an Immigration Judge pretermitted his application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. This appeal followed.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). These proceedings fall within the REAL ID Act.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in pretermitting his application for adjustment of status. He acknowledges that the visa petition filed on his behalf by his spouse was denied by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). According to the respondent, the denial is being appealed to the Board, and the Immigration Judge erred by not continuing the proceedings until a decision was rendered on the appeal. However, our records indicate that the appeal of the visa petition denial was dismissed by the Board on February 23, 2011. The respondent is therefore ineligible for a grant of adjustment of status because he is not the beneficiary of an approved visa petition. The issue of whether the Immigration Judge correctly denied the continuance request is thus now moot. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
We note that the respondent raises other issues on appeal relating to the reason his current counsel did not timely respond to the motion to pretermitt. We need not address whether the respondent may have been a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel inasmuch as the above determination is dispositive.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent filed a motion to reopen after the Immigration Judge pretermitted his adjustment of status application. However, the motion was not adjudicated because the respondent appealed the order pretermitting his application before the Immigration Judge had the opportunity to decide the motion.

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Administrative closure, continuance
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Germany, and a lawful permanent resident of the United States since October 29, 1956, has filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 19, 2012, denying his request that his proceedings be continued or administratively closed while he pursued post-conviction relief. The respondent’s request to proceed on appeal in forma pauperis is granted under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3). See Matter of Chicas, 19 I&N Dec. 114 (BIA 1984). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance or administrative closure so that he may pursue post-conviction relief. The Immigration Judge properly denied the request for failure to demonstrate good cause because the outcome of any efforts to obtain post-conviction relief, including a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), would be speculative, and the respondent’s conviction is considered final for immigration purposes unless and until it is overturned.1 See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240; Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n.8 (BIA 2009) (“A pending collateral attack does not disturb the finality of a conviction and therefore would not justify reopening of removal proceedings.”); Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992) (“the possibility of a decision on any post-conviction motion that has been filed does not affect our finding that the respondent is deportable.”). See Matter of Avetisyan, 25 I&N Dec. 688, 696 (BIA 2012) (“[I]t would not be appropriate to administratively close proceedings if the request is based on a purely speculative event or action... or an event or action that may or may not affect the course of an alien’s immigration proceedings (such as a collateral attack on a criminal conviction.”)
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Sharon C Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We point out that the respondent admitted the allegation stating that, subsequent to the Supreme Court’s decision in Padilla, his convictions were upheld by the Court of Appeals for the State of Oregon. Further, more than 4 months have elapsed since the Immigration Judge rendered his decision in this case, and the respondent has not provided information that he has prevailed in his collateral criminal proceedings.
