Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELISEO PEREYRA
File: AXXX XX4 954 - Cleveland, OH
July 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret W. Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael A. Tripi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 29, 2011. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. We find that the respondent’s contention that his in absentia order of removal should be vacated because he had departed the United States prior to his removal hearing is without merit. In Matter of Brown, 18 I&N Dec. 324, 325 (BIA 1982), the Board held that an alien “cannot compel the termination of deportation proceedings which have been commenced against him merely by effecting a departure and reentry.” Once jurisdiction was vested with the Immigration Judge by the filing of the Notice to Appear (Form I-862), the Immigration Judge obtained jurisdiction and the respondent remained subject to removal proceedings, even though he departed the United States and re-entered. Hence, when the respondent did not appear for his scheduled removal hearing, we find that the Immigration Judge properly entered an order of removal.1
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that his failure to appear for his hearing was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C) (requiring that motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances be filed within 180 days of the date of the order of removal). Moreover, the respondent has not fully complied with the procedural requirements for demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).2
 
Furthermore, we find that the respondent has not established that his motion should be granted pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). The respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

To the extent that the respondent references an unpublished Board decision finding to the contrary, we note that this Board is not bound by its unpublished decisions.

2

We would apply similar reasoning affirming the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen if the respondent had simply been ordered removed instead of being ordered removed in absentia. The respondent’s motion to reopen was not filed within 90 days of his order of removal and the respondent has not demonstrated that his motion is subject to any exception to the filing deadline. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was ordered removed in absentia on June 9, 2011. On June 13, 2011, the respondent filed a motion to reopen proceedings, which the Immigration Judge denied on August 15, 2011. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened and the record will be remanded.
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that he had received proper notice for his June 9, 2011, hearing. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied.
 
Upon review, we find that in view of the above and in light of the totality of circumstances presented in this case, including evidence reflecting that the respondent’s counsel conceded that he provided ineffective assistance by failing to appear at the June 9, 2011, hearing, we will allow the respondent another opportunity to appear for a hearing.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Sharon C Hoffman

MOTION

William Pryor, Esquire

Angela Marra
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case has been the subject of two prior Board decisions. In the Board’s decision on September 21, 2011, the appeal was dismissed, and a motion to remand based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was denied. In denying the motion to remand, the Board found that there had been no compliance with the threshold procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, noting that more than one year had elapsed since the appeal was filed. On March 9, 2012, the Board denied a motion for reconsideration of the September 21, 2011, decision and a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance before the Immigration Judge, finding that the respondent and current counsel could have pursued an ineffective assistance of counsel claim with Lozada compliance before the Immigration Judge or on appeal. See Tr. at 12-13. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider the Board’s decisions on March 9, 2012, and September 21, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2), only one motion to reconsider a Board decision is permitted, and a party may not seek reconsideration of a decision denying a previous motion to reconsider. Consequently, the respondent’s second motion for reconsideration of the September 21, 2011, decision, and motion for reconsideration of the March 9, 2012, denial of reconsideration will be denied. The respondent also has not shown that reconsideration of the March 2012 denial of reopening is warranted, as there is no legal or factual defect in the decision denying reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The respondent’s tactical decision to delay pursuit of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim and/or compliance with the procedural threshold requirements for such a claim is not tantamount to showing that evidence in support of such a claim could not have been obtained and presented at the hearing below. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
ORDER: The motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDDIE MENDIOLA
File: AXXX XX9 498 - Aurora, CO
July 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stacy Tolchin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nathan L. Herbert
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. This case was last before the Board on October 7, 2008, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The final administrative order was entered in these proceedings on November 9, 2004, when the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The current motion to reopen was filed on March 28, 2012, and two prior motions to reopen where denied by the Board in 2007 and 2008. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a brief in opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent is seeking reopening and termination of his removal proceedings or cancellation of removal under section 240A(a)of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), but such a motion does not fall within any exception to the time and numerical limits for a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3) (setting forth exceptions to the time and number limits). To the extent that the respondent is raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney, he has not established prejudice such that equitable tolling of the time and number limits is warranted. See Akinwunmi v. INS, 194 F.3d 1340, 1341 n.2 (10th Cir. 1999). While the respondent urges that his prior counsel should have argued that he was not removable pursuant to the Board’s decision in Matter of Paulus, 11 I&N Dec. 274 (BIA 1965), the respondent has not established that the specific controlled substance of “steroids” was not listed on the Federal controlled substances schedule for both 1996 and 2000. The respondent’s reliance on the current controlled substances schedules to assert that the California and Federal controlled substances schedules do not coincide, is misplaced. As such, the respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s actions or inactions such that equitable tolling of the time and number limits is warranted.
 
The respondent further urges that he warrants sua sponte reopening because he is no longer removable as an aggravated felon pursuant to the United States Supreme Court’s decision in Carachurri-Rosendo v. Holder, 130 S.Ct. 2577 (2010). He further asserts that in light of this recent case law, his removal proceedings should be reopened and terminated or in the alternative that he is statutorily eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), despite his involuntary removal from the United States on March 2, 2005.1 However, the respondent does not contest that he was removable as an aggravated felon at the time of his removal from the United States in 2005 (Motion at 13). As such, the respondent’s removal from the United States in March 2005 was lawful and this removal served to terminate the respondent’s lawful permanent residence status. See Matter of Lok, 18 I&N Dec. 101, 106 (BIA 1981); Matter of Mosqueda, 14 I&N Dec. 55, 56-57 (R.C. 1972). Consequently, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act because he is no longer lawfully admitted for permanent residence. See section 240A(a)(1) of the Act. For the same reason, termination of the respondent’s removal proceedings is not appropriate. A decision of this Board granting the respondent’s motion would not restore him to his prior lawful permanent resident status, moreover, because such an order is merely an interlocutory measure authorizing the submission of new evidence; it does not abrogate the existing removal order or confer lawful immigration status on the movant. Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349, 354 (BIA 1998). Since the respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for any immediately available forms of relief, we do not find reopening is warranted. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s departure pursuant to his March 2, 2005, removal precludes merit consideration of his untimely and number-barred motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d). As the respondent concedes the untimeliness of his third motion and its inability to fall within any exception to the time and number limitations, he has not demonstrated that the departure bar would not apply to his motion. See Contreras-Bocanegra v. Holder, 629 F.3d 1170 (10th Cir. 2010), rev’d en banc, 678 F.3d 811 (10th Cir. 2012).

IN RE: DESIREE ANN INGRAM A.K.A. DESIREE ANN DAKERS

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The final administrative order of removal in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 31, 2006. On April 27, 2012, alleging ineffective of former counsels, the respondent, a native and citizen of Belize, filed the pending motion to reopen. However, the motion is untimely filed and has not been shown to qualify for any exception to the filing requirements imposed on motions to reopen removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Moreover, we decline to equitably toll the applicable filing deadlines. The respondent does not specifically contend that she was unaware of the Board’s August 31, 2006, decision. Nor does she specially assert or indicate in any way that the alleged action or inaction of former counsels prevented her from timely filing the instant motion. Indeed, she does not dispute that she was aware of the alleged deficient performances of former counsels prior to the Board’s August 31, 2006, decision. Under the circumstances, particularly where, as here, first former counsel describes the respondent’s lack of cooperation in pursuing her claim, we do not find that the respondent has sufficiently demonstrated that she received ineffective assistance. Nor has she sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against each of her former counsels, including the requirement that she show prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). Nor has there been any showing of due diligence in pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra.
 
On this record, the respondent has not provided us with an adequate reason to excuse the untimeliness of her motion. Nor has she demonstrated any exceptional circumstance that would warrant the reopening of these proceedings under the Board’s discretionary sua sponte authority. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion and related request for a stay of removal are denied.1
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

However, the respondent’s appellate fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3).

IN RE: JOSE CABRERA-LEON
File: AXXX XX5 507 - New York, NY
June 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bibiana C. Andrade, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela Marra
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On October 31, 2011, the Board entered the final administrative order in this case, and on February 29, 2012, the Board denied the respondent’s previous motion to reopen. On March 29, 2012, the respondent filed the instant untimely, number-barred motion to reopen. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent now raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). He alleges prejudicial error on the part of his former attorney because she entered pleading on his behalf, thereby admitting to his being removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (present without being admitted or paroled), thus precluding his eligibility for adjustment of status. He specifically argues that his former attorney should have denied the allegation that the respondent is an alien present in the United States without being admitted and paroled because he was paroled into the United States pursuant to a grant of advanced parole under section 212(d)(5)(A) of the Act.
 
It is undisputed that the respondent first entered the United States without inspection on or about March 15, 1999, as alleged in the Notice to Appear, and that he was subsequently authorized to be paroled upon his return to the United States to pursue a pending application for adjustment of status. The fact that the respondent was paroled into the United States pursuant to a grant of advanced parole does not, under the circumstances, render the respondent admissible. See Ibragimov v. Gonzales, 476 F.3d 125 (2nd Cir. 2007); Matter of G-A-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 83, 88-89 (BIA 1998) (explaining that section 212(d)(5) of the Act makes clear that the parole shall not be regarded as an admission of the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 212.5(e)(2)(i). In the present case, the respondent was properly placed in removal proceedings and charged with inadmissibility following the denial by the DHS of his application for adjustment of status on August 8, 2003, and the revocation of his parole.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rabiu v. I.N.S., 41 F.3d 879, 882-83 (2nd Cir. 1994); Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. Therefore, equitable tolling of the filing restrictions imposed on this motion is not warranted. See Iavorski v. U.S. I.N.S., 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). Further, it does not appear that any exception to the filing restrictions applies to this motion or that any exceptional situation is present in this case to warrant the exercise of the Board’s sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(a), (c)(3). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2834968 (BIA)

2012 WL 2835215 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR OROBIO
File: AXXX XX8 607 - Newark, NJ
June 19, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Ines Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia M. Medeiros
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on July 20, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his request for a remand based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. This motion, filed on February 8, 2012, is not timely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security opposes reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We construe this as an argument that the time limits on motions should be equitably tolled. The time limits on motions may be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance where an alien establishes that he was prejudiced by the misconduct, and where he has shown due diligence in discovering and correcting the errors. See Alzaarir v. Att’y Gen., 639 F.3d 86, 90 (3d Cir. 2011).
 
We are not persuaded that equitable tolling is warranted in this case.
 
First, the purported ineffective assistance all occurred at the trial level, and as the respondent knew of the ostensible failures of his prior counsel at that time the untimeliness of this motion is not related to the ineffective assistance. Accordingly, equitable tolling does not apply.
 
Second, the respondent has not shown due diligence in this case. He previously raised these arguments of ineffective assistance during the course of his appeal 6 years ago and requested a remand. We denied the respondent’s request for a remand because he failed to submit the necessary corroborative evidence. The respondent has not provided any explanation for why he waited over 5 years to submit another motion.
 
The respondent’s motion does not substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He has not provided evidence of his agreement with his former attorney, he has not indicated whether he has submitted a bar complaint, and there is no evidence that the former attorney has been notified of these allegations. The respondent’s arguments relating to ineffective assistance rest primarily on his former attorney’s suspension, seemingly in an unrelated matter, which does not establish that there was ineffective assistance in this case.
 
Finally, the respondent has not included the documents which his prior attorney should ostensibly have obtained and submitted. Without being able to examine the proffered evidence, we are unable to conclude that the respondent was prejudiced in any respect.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2835215 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2012 WL 2835217 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHIRLAND MARCIA MCDONALD
File: AXXX XX7 840 - New York, NY
June 18, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Genet Getachew, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision issued June 27, 2011, denying her request to reopen proceedings after she was ordered removed in absentia.1 The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a brief or other response. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including credibility findings, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews de novo all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges, including legal and discretionary determinations and applications of law to fact. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at the February 8, 2011, hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). The respondent on appeal has not challenged the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not comply with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See generally Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (finding that ineffective assistance of counsel may amount to “exceptional circumstances” in the context of a motion to reopen an in absentia removal order).2 The respondent has not identified the individual whom she claims purported to be able to assist her, or whether or not the individual was an attorney or otherwise authorized to represent her under section 8 C.F.R. § 1292.1. The record does not contain a Form EOIR-28, Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative before the Immigration Court, or other evidence of representation or agreement with the respondent. To the extent the respondent’s attorney on appeal makes general assertions about the individual and the respondent’s experiences, statements of counsel are not evidence. See, e.g., INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
In the event that the individual was a non-attorney, the extent to which the Matter of Lozada requirements are mandated is an unsettled question, but some compliance has been required, generally of the first two steps. See Omar v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 647, 651 (2d Cir. 2008); Matter of Zmijewska, 24 I&N Dec. 87, 94-95 (BIA 2007) (finding that the respondent satisfied the concerns underlying Matter of Lozada where she submitted to the Board a copy of a formal complaint provided to her accredited representative, whose representation and specific action had been described to the Board, and apparently offered the accredited representative an opportunity to respond). In contrast, the respondent in the instant matter has not identified the individual or the capacity in which he or she presented his or her ability to assist the respondent, the nature of the agreement between the individual and the respondent, any proof of payment, what specific representations the individual made to the respondent about her immigration proceedings, or what the individual said, if anything, specifically about the February 8, 2011, hearing at which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. The respondent has also not provided evidence that a formal complaint was provided to the individual with an opportunity to respond.
 
*2 Furthermore, the respondent has not established that she was prejudiced by the individual’s actions. See Omar v. Mukasey, supra, at 51 (finding no error where the Board determined that the respondent had presented neither sufficient compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, to whatever extent it may be required for non-attorneys, nor prejudice from the alleged actions); see also Matter of Assaad, supra, at 561-62. The respondent has not demonstrated that she would have attended the February 8, 2011, hearing but for the advice of the individual. We note that the respondent, to whom the Notice to Appear with the time, date, and location of the Immigration Court hearing was mailed, has not argued that she did not have notice of the hearing (Exh. 1). The respondent’s statement provided with the motion to reopen does not indicate that she was advised to not attend the hearing. Moreover, the respondent has not contested the ground of removability under section 237(a)(1)(D)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i), nor demonstrated eligibility for any form of relief, including voluntary departure which she mentioned briefly in the motion to reopen. We therefore conclude that the respondent has not shown prejudice based upon the alleged ineffectiveness of the unidentified individual.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Counsel for the respondent has not filed a separate written brief or statement, despite indicating on the Notice of Appeal that a brief would be submitted and despite seeking and receiving a briefing extension. We considered the statements on the Notice of Appeal as the basis of the respondent’s appeal, although they lack specificity. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(A), (E) (permitting summary dismissal in express situations, including failure to file a brief as indicated).

2

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by (1) an affidavit setting out the agreement entered into with counsel, (2) proof that notice of the allegations and an opportunity to respond has been provided to counsel whose representation was allegedly ineffective, and (3) the filing of a complaint with appropriate disciplinary authorities or an explanation for the failure to file a complaint. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-62 (BIA 2003) (requiring prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions in addition to compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra).

2012 WL 2835224 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR MANUEL RAMIREZ-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX0 382 - Los Angeles, CA
June 14, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vera A. Weisz, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
In a decision dated May 10, 2011, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Mexico after determining that he had not been cooperating with his attorney and was not eligible for relief other than voluntary departure; the Immigration Judge denied voluntary departure as a matter of discretion. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that his prior attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge failed to explore all of the respondent’s immigration remedies and poorly advised him, including telling him to inform the Immigration Judge that he needed more time because he was getting married, when in reality he was not (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 3, 6-8). He argues that his former attorney did not raise the issue of the respondent qualifying for a U visa and his counselor withdrew from the case when the respondent refused to make a false claim of political asylum (Respondent’s Br. at 6, 8).
 
The respondent does not claim to have met the procedural requirements for establishing a prima facie case of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (stating that in order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish and include the following: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not); see also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (stating that the Lozada requirements “insures that the record contains the ‘essential information’ necessary to ‘evaluate the substance’ of the alien’s claim”).
 
While the respondent is correct that strict compliance with Lozada’s procedural requirements for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is not required in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, particularly where the record reflects a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” (Respondent’s Br. at 6), we do not find this to be the case here. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). The fact that the respondent did not comply with the Lozada requirements precludes any presumption that the respondent was provided with ineffective assistance of counsel. Additionally, although the respondent alleges that his prior counsel told him to lie to the Immigration Judge about getting married and advised him to file a false asylum application, there is no proof that his prior counsel was actively engaging in fraud. With such allegations, it would be particularly helpful if the respondent did notify his counsel of these allegations and submitted a response from the attorney in order to facilitate the evaluation of such conduct.
 
*2 Nevertheless, there is no evidence that the respondent was prejudiced by his former attorney’s actions such that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case or from applying for relief which would have been available to him. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that ineffective assistance of counsel results in a denial of due process under the Fifth Amendment only when the proceeding is so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonable presenting his case); see also Rodriguez-Gonzalez v. INS, 640 F.2d 1139, 1142 (9th Cir. 1981) (stating that legal strategies which are unwise in hindsight do not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent has not established that the proceedings would have been different but for his counsel’s alleged unprofessional errors. The respondent’s prior counsel appears to have asked to withdraw from representing the respondent during a preliminary hearing but was asked to remain of counsel to give the respondent another opportunity to file applications for relief (Tr. at 17-19). At the next hearing, the respondent still had not presented any applications, after being explicitly informed by the Immigration Judge that this was his last continuance for filing applications (Tr. at 20-21), and his attorney again alleged a lack of cooperation as grounds for seeking withdrawal (Tr. at 22). The Immigration Judge then found the respondent’s applications abandoned and granted the respondent’s counsel’s request to withdraw (Tr. at 23, 25; I.J. at 2).
 
The respondent does not dispute that he did not submit any applications for relief when asked to by the Immigration Judge, and on appeal, he does not allege or establish that he is eligible for any relief. Therefore, we cannot conclude that, even assuming his attorney ineffective, there was prejudice. Although the respondent claims that his prior counsel should have established his eligibility for a U visa based on being a victim of a crime (Respondent’s Br. at 1-2, 8), the record does not contain sufficient evidence that the respondent would qualify for such a visa or that he has even applied for it. At any rate, we do not have jurisdiction to grant such relief. See Matter of Sanchez Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012) (discussing how an alien establishes prima facie eligibility for a U visa and whether a remand or continuance is warranted to await adjudication of such relief).
 
Likewise, although the respondent asserts that he is eligible for “prosecutorial discretion” (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 8), the Board and the Immigration Judges do not have authority to influence or require the Department of Homeland Security to exercise its discretion in deciding which cases to prosecute or not. See Reno v. American Arab Anti-Discrimination Committee, 525 U.S. 471, 488-91 (1999); Lopez-Telles v. INS, 564 F.2d 1302, 1304 (9th Cir. 1977). Although cancellation of removal was raised as a possible form of relief for which the respondent might be eligible (Tr. at 15), it was ultimately not pursued, and the respondent does not appear statutorily eligible for such relief (see Tr. at 24). See section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). Finally, it is not clear whether the respondent is statutorily eligible for voluntary departure, which the Immigration Judge denied as a matter of discretion (Tr. at 23; I.J. at 2), but the respondent does not raise this issue on appeal and, therefore, we decline to address it further.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 2835227 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLIVIA BUKSH CORTEZ A.K.A. OLIVIA BUKAH CORTEZ
File: AXXX XX7 192 - Baltimore, Maryland
June 14, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kai W. Degraaf, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carrie E. Johnston
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Termination
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s January 18, 2011, oral decision which found her subject to removal as charged. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a response on appeal in support of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including the credibility determination, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2012). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues involving questions of law, judgement and discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent, a 55-year-old native and citizen of Philippines, entered the United States in 2003 on a tourist’s visa. She eventually was hired as a live-in nanny by a United States citizen who led her to believe that she would assist her in becoming lawful permanent resident with the assistance of two prior attorneys. However, the petition for alien worker eventually was denied, and a Notice to Appear was issued in March 2009, which alleged that the respondent was subject to removal because she had overstayed her tourist visa. The respondent, through the same attorney who represents her on appeal, conceded the factual allegations and removability on August 31, 2010 (Tr. at 8).
 
On December 7, 2010, the respondent, through counsel, submitted a motion to terminate proceedings setting forth the preceding facts. He alleged that the respondent had been deprived of due process essentially because she had been misled by her employer and the prior attorneys and that the Notice to Appear had been “improvidently issued,” citing 8 C.F.R. § 239.2(a)(6). On December 8, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the motion via a preprinted order in which he had handwritten that the respondent “has already admitted the allegations and conceded removability as charged.”
 
At a hearing on January 18, 2011, the respondent, through counsel, requested a final order of removal in order that he could pursue the possibility of deferred action, and he conceded that the respondent was not eligible for any form of relief. Therefore, the Immigration Judge entered an order of removal on January 18, 2011.
 
The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal which indicated that she was appealing the Immigration Judge’s removal order of January 18, 2011. However, counsel has submitted a brief on appeal which reiterates the arguments contained in the motion to terminate and alleges again that the Notice to Appear was improvidently issued. The respondent essentially argues that she should not have been placed in removal proceedings because she received ineffective assistance of counsel, without any legal support for this position. In addition, she maintains that she should not be required to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) because the ineffective assistance in her case is plainly apparent.
 
*2 The respondent has misinterpreted the regulations and case law which permit the DHS or an Immigration Judge to cancel a Notice to Appear as improvidently issued. The regulations and case law do not permit an alien to request that a Notice to Appear be cancelled on this basis. See generally Matter of W-C-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 118 (BIA 2007); Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 239.2(a)(6), (c).
 
In the circumstances, we will affirm the Immigration Judge’s decisions of December 8, 2010, which denied the motion to terminate, and January 18, 2011, which ordered the respondent removed. The respondent was represented by competent counsel who conceded the factual allegations and removability, she has not established that she is eligible for any form of relief, or that she is not subject to removal as charged.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2012 WL 2835227 (BIA)

2012 WL 2835232 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS BAUTISTA-RAMIREZ
File: AXXX XX3 571 - San Antonio, TX
June 13, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Teresa Martinez-Alonso, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christina Playton
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; “vacate the execution of an order of removal;” stay of removal; change of venue
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated April 12, 2012, denying “Motion to Reconsider [,] Emergency Motion to Reopen, to Vacate the Execution of an Order of Removal, and Request for Stay of Removal and to Change Venue.” We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
After the respondent was served with a Notice to Appear in 2007, the Immigration Court sent a Notice of Hearing (“NOH”) to the respondent’s former counsel, Pascual Madrigal, informing him that a removal hearing had been scheduled on March 10, 2008. As the respondent did not appear at that hearing, he was ordered removed in absentia under the provisions of section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A).
 
On March 7, 2012, nearly 4 years after he was ordered removed in absentia, the respondent, through his present counsel, filed an “Emergency Motion to Reopen, to Vacate the Execution of an Order of Removal, and Request for Stay of Removal and to Change Venue.” While the respondent argued that he did not receive the NOH, he did not address the fact that the NOH was actually sent to Mr. Madrigal. As such, on March 19, 2012, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion was untimely under the general 90-day filing deadline governing motions to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act. Moreover, rescission of the order of removal entered against the respondent was not warranted on the basis of lack of notice as notice to an alien’s counsel of record satisfies the notice requirements set forth in section 239(a)(2)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(2)(A). See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. The respondent did not allege that Mr. Madrigal did not receive the NOH. As the respondent did not timely appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion, his decision is now final. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38, 1003.39.
 
On April 3, 2012, the respondent filed a “Motion to Reconsider[,] Emergency Motion to Reopen, to Vacate the Execution of an Order of Removal, and Request for Stay of Removal and to Change Venue.” In his motion, the respondent argued that he was “forced” to file the prior motion due to his imminent removal, and, for the first time, argued that Mr. Madrigal engaged in ineffective assistance of counsel by not informing the respondent of his March 2008 removal hearing. For the reasons set forth below, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent sought reconsideration of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the initial motion, the respondent has not demonstrated errors of law or fact in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). As discussed above, the respondent’s initial “Emergency Motion to Reopen, to Vacate the Execution of an Order of Removal, and Request for Stay of Removal and to Change Venue” was properly denied as it was untimely and the respondent had not demonstrated that rescission of the order of removal was warranted on the basis of lack of notice. While the respondent did raise, for the first time, a claim to reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, such claims did not warrant reconsideration. It is well-established that a motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied.1 See Omari v. Holder, 562 F.3d 314, 319 (5th Cir. 2009) (“A motion for reconsideration is thus confined to the substance of the [Board’s] original decision.”); Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58 (citing Matter of Medrano, 20 I&N Dec. 216, 219 (BIA 1990, 1991), for the proposition that “arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion”). A motion to reconsider is not a mechanism by which a party may raise completely new arguments. The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims were not properly raised in the context of a motion to reconsider as they did not “flow” from any arguments raised in his initial motion to reopen. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 57.
 
To the extent that the respondent sought reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, the Immigration Judge properly concluded that such claims were number-barred (I.J. at 1). Section 240(c)(7)(C)(A) of the Act and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) provide an alien with a single opportunity to file a motion to reopen seeking to rescind of an order of removal entered in absentia. As the respondent used that opportunity when he filed his “Emergency Motion to Reopen, to Vacate the Execution of an Order of Removal, and Request for Stay of Removal and to Change Venue” on March 7, 2012, the respondent’s second motion, which is the subject of the current appeal, was number-barred. The respondent’s claim that he was “forced” to file his original motion does not amount to a cognizable basis to toll the clear statutory and regulatory limit to filing one motion to reopen on an equitable basis. We observe that, even though the respondent claimed that Mr. Madrigal was ineffective with respect to the respondent’s underlying removal proceedings, the respondent has not alleged that the attorney who filed his initial motion provided ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, we conclude that the respondent’s claims against Mr. Madrigal are number-barred. We decline to equitably toll the applicable numerical limit governing the respondent’s reopening claims.
 
*3 As neither the respondent’s reconsideration nor reopening claim was meritorious, his additional requests to “vacate the execution of an order of removal,” stay his removal, and change venue were also without merit. We observe that an alien seeking to vacate an order of removal under the provisions of section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act must bring such claims “upon a motion to reopen” as discussed above, the respondent’s second request for reopening was number-barred. Additionally, a change of venue was not warranted as no additional removal hearing are required in this matter.
 
Since taking this appeal, the respondent has filed additional evidence with this Board. Much of the evidence was previously filed with the Immigration Judge. Moreover, we do not consider evidence submitted on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (“The Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record.”). We also do not consider a party’s arguments which are raised for the first time on appeal. See Matter of Jimenez, 21 I&N Dec. 567, 570 n.2 (BIA 1996) (noting that an issue which was neither raised before, nor ruled upon by the Immigration Judge, is not properly before us).
 
To the extent that the respondent’s additional evidence can be considered a motion to remand to the Immigration Judge to consider such evidence, the respondent’s claims are numerically barred as an alien is permitted an opportunity to file a single motion to reopen and a single motion to reconsider. See sections 240(c)(b)(A), (7)(A). In this case, the respondent has used both of those opportunities. As such, a request for a remand to consider the evidence in not permitted. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000) (recognizing that motions to remand filed after an administrative order has been issued are simply motions to reopen, and they are subject to the same numerical limitations as other motions to reopen).
 
Finally, to the extent that the respondent’s claims concerning his equities in this country, the ability to obtain relief from removal, and his ineffective assistance of counsel claims, can be construed as a request for sua sponte reconsideration or reopening, the respondent has not demonstrated that such measures are warranted in this case. The power to reconsider or reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
For the reasons set forth above, we will not disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s “Motion to Reconsider[,] Emergency Motion to Reopen, to Vacate the Execution of an Order of Removal, and Request for Stay of Removal and to Change Venue.”
 
*4 In light of this decision, we will vacate the stay of removal which was previously granted. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 108 (BIA 2009) (recognizing that the limited stay authority provides the opportunity to stay proceedings while a pending motion is adjudicated and terminates upon adjudication of the pending motion). At the present time, the order of removal entered against the respondent on March 10, 2008, by the Immigration Judge, serves as a valid basis upon which the respondent can be removed from this country. If the respondent seeks a stay of removal on the basis of humanitarian concerns, such a request must be made with the Department of Homeland Security. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6. Accordingly, the following orders are entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The stay of removal entered by this Board on April 17, 2012, is vacated.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Outside of the context of immigration proceedings, the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has also recognized that motions to reconsider cannot be used to raise arguments which could, and should, have been made before the judgment issued. Williams v. Toyota Motor Engineering & Mfg. North America, Inc., No. 11-60593, 2012 WL 1521640 (May 1, 2012); Rosenzweig v. Azurix Corp., 332 F.3d 854, 863 (5th Cir. 2003).

2012 WL 2835234 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILKINS YEREMIS DE LOS SANTOS
File: AXXX XX6 570 - York, PA
June 12, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Salvador Delgado, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jon D. Staples
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 28, 2012, decision ordering his removal. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent has also filed a motion to reopen, which we construe as a motion to remand. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1999). We review findings of fact by the Immigration Judge for clear error, while all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
The respondent concedes that he was convicted of criminal sale of marijuana in violation of New York Penal Law § 221.40 (2004) and of unlawful possession of marijuana in violation of New York Penal Law § 221.05 (2004). However, the respondent challenges the validity of these convictions, and argues that the Immigration Judge should not have relied on such convictions in sustaining the above charges of removability. The respondent also challenges the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he has been convicted of an aggravated felony.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), as an alien who has any time after admission been convicted of a controlled substance violation, other than a single offense involving possession for one’s own use of 30 grams or less of marijuana. Although the respondent argues on appeal that he was not guilty as charged of the marijuana sale offense, it is well settled that neither an Immigration Judge nor this Board may look behind the fact of conviction and re-litigate the ultimate question of an alien’s guilt or innocence. See Matter of Roberts, 20 I&N Dec. 294 (BIA 1991). Similarly, although the respondent may assert that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during criminal proceedings, the respondent does not indicate that he has successfully pursued a collateral challenge to this offense in state court, and the conviction therefore supports a finding of removability in these proceedings. See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992). Finally, although the respondent states that his convictions may have been expunged from his criminal record pursuant to a rehabilitative statute, an alien remains convicted of an offense for immigration purposes pursuant to the definition of “conviction” set forth in section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A), notwithstanding a subsequent vacatur under a state rehabilitative statute. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). For these reasons, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent has been convicted of two controlled substance violations and is removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act.1
 
*2 We do not find merit in the respondent’s Motion to Reopen Deportation Proceedings.2 The respondent’s motion was initially filed with the Immigration Court during the pendency of this appeal. It was properly rejected by the Immigration Court because an Immigration Judge ceases to have jurisdiction over a motion to reopen at such time as jurisdiction vests with this Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). A “motion to reopen” is not properly before the Board as the respondent’s motion was filed during the pendency of the present appeal, and thus at a time when there was no administratively final order of removal in his case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1241.1. To the extent that we therefore construe the respondent’s motion as a motion to remand, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated that remand is warranted. See Matter of Coelho, supra. While it appears that the respondent argues in his motion that he was inappropriately ordered removed in absentia, we note that the respondent was present and participated at his February 28, 2012, hearing. Further, although the respondent appears to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has not submitted evidence demonstrating compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (enumerating evidentiary requirements for a party to prevail upon an ineffective assistance of counsel claim). For these reasons, we conclude that the respondent has not shown that remand is warranted in this case. The respondent’s request for a stay or removal is moot, and the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

1

Because we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, we conclude that we need not reach the Immigration Judge’s aggravated felony determination. We note that the respondent, who was admitted in 2002, cannot establish eligibility for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), because his 2004 convictions preclude him from accruing the required continuous residence. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act.

2012 WL 2835240 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JERMANINE RONNIE SALMON A.K.A. JERMAINE R SALMON A.K.A. TIKI SALMON A.K.A. TIKI R SALMON
File: AXXX XX2 933 - Newark, NJ
June 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Continuance; voluntary departure
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 8, 2011, denying his request for a continuance and finding him removable as charged.1 During the pendency of this appeal, the respondent submitted new evidence that we construe as a motion to remand. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The respondent’s request for a waiver of the filing fees associated with the appeal is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1), 1003.8. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.2
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, including any determination of credibility, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have continued the proceedings since his post-conviction relief (“PCR”) was pending. He further argues that he entered defective guilty pleas due to ineffective assistance from his criminal counsel below, in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, ___ U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct 1473 (2010).3 The respondent contends that his constitutional rights were violated.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately went forward with the proceedings without granting the respondent an additional continuance (I.J. at 2-4; Tr. at 4-6 dated Mar. 8, 2011). The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, and good cause must be shown for a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Khan v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., 448 F.3d 226, 233 (3d Cir. 2006); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 355-57 (BIA 1983). In this case, the Immigration Judge had continued the proceedings from December 14, 2010, until March 8, 2011, based on the respondent’s testimony that his PCR case for the 2008 conviction had been granted (I.J. at 2-3; see, e.g., Tr. at 31-33 dated Jan. 3, 2011). However, at the March 2011 hearing, the respondent proffered evidence indicating that criminal counsel had been appointed and his PCR case was pending (I.J. at 2-3; Tr. at 1-3 dated Mar. 8, 2011). The record also reflects that the respondent intended to pursue PCR for his 2007 conviction (Tr. at 5 dated Mar. 8, 2011). Thus, as the respondent’s ability to prevail on his PCR case was speculative, the Immigration Judge appropriately denied his request for a continuance. To date, the respondent has not proffered evidence to suggest that his PCR case for the 2008 conviction will be successful.
 
*2 Further, the fact that the respondent may be pursuing PCR in the form of a collateral attack for his 2008 conviction in state criminal court does not affect its finality for federal immigration purposes. See Paredes v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., 528 F.3d 196, 198-99 (3d Cir. 2008) (stating the pendency of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attacks does not vitiate finality, unless and until the convictions are overturned as a result of the collateral motion); see also Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999). The respondent did not present evidence with this appeal that any attack on his 2008 conviction resulted in any vacatur. Likewise, there is no evidence that the respondent’s 2007 conviction has been vacated. In Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, the United States Supreme Court ruled that an attorney representing an alien in connection with a guilty plea to a criminal offense has a constitutional duty to advise the alien about the risk of deportation arising from the conviction. However, the respondent’s appellate assertions that his convictions are invalid, in light of Padilla v. Kentucky, supra, does not change the finality of his convictions for immigration purposes, unless and until they are overturned by the criminal court.4 See Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992); Matter of Ponce De Leon-Ruiz, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 157 (A.G. 1997; BIA 1996). In other words, we are bound by the conviction records and we have no authority to vacate the convictions before us as the judgements do not appear to be a void on their face.
 
The record also does not support the respondent’s contentions that his constitutional rights were violated. To establish a violation of due process, the respondent must demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced from the alleged procedural errors. See Leslie v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., 611 F.3d 171, 175-76 (3d Cir. 2010); Khan v. Att’y Gen. of the U.S., supra, at 236. Here, the respondent was afforded a fair hearing to present his claims for relief. The Immigration Judge had continued the proceedings to determine the status of the respondent’s PCR case. The Immigration Judge had also determined that the respondent was statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal, that he did not express any fears of persecution or torture if removed to Jamaica, and that he had misplaced his passport and was unable to pay for his travel (Tr. at 3-4, 6 dated Mar. 8, 2011). Based on this record, the respondent did not demonstrate that he was substantially prejudiced by the Immigration Judge, and therefore, we find no violation of due process.
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
To the extent the respondent seeks to apply for voluntary departure under section 240B(b), 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”) and proffers new evidence, we construe it as a motion to remand and we deny the motion. Motions to remand are adjudicated under the same standards as are motions to reopen. See Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880, 884 (BIA 1994). Such a motion may not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). A motion to remand must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen where the respondent seeks to present new evidence, but will only be granted if the new evidence “would likely change the result in the case.” See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992).
 
Although the respondent now asserts that his fiancee holds his passport and that his family can pay for his travel, he has not established by clear and convincing evidence that he has the means to depart the United States and intends to do so. See section 240B(b)(D) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c). The respondent bears the burden of proof to establish eligibility for voluntary departure. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.8(d); 1240.26(c). Aside from the respondent’s conclusory statements, the proffered statements from the respondents’s brother, cousin, father, grandmother, uncle, co-worker, and friends, do not demonstrate, by clear and convincing evidence, that the respondent has the financial means to depart voluntarily and intends to do so. See also Matter of Arguelles-Campos, 20 I&N Dec. 811 (BIA 1999). Rather, the proffered statements suggest that the respondent seeks another opportunity to remain in the United States. In his most recent submission filed with the Board on May 4, 2012, the respondent did not present any further evidence to establish that he is prima facie eligible for voluntary departure. Thus, we will deny the respondent’s motion.
 
Due to the nature of our disposition of this matter, we need not address the respondent’s other arguments on appeal and we find no basis to remand this case for further proceedings. See INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (stating as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 465 (BIA 2002). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*4 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD

2

We consider these issues to be waived. See Lie v. Ashcroft, 396 F.3d 530, 532 n.1 (3d Cir. 2005); Matter of J-Y-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 260, 261 n.1 (BIA 2007); Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196-97 n.4 (BIA 1990).

IN RE: WAN SOO CHEE KUM NAM CHEE SUNG AE CHEE BONG SUK CHEE
Files: AXXX XX3 669 - San Francisco, CA
AXXX XX3 670
AXXX XX3 671
AXXX XX3 672
June 5, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Michael T. Purcell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Grace H. Cheung
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents are natives and citizens of South Korea. This proceeding involved Leland Sustaire, a former federal government employee who obtained lawful permanent resident status for a number of aliens, including the respondents here, even though there was no underlying basis for such status (Exhs. 4-5). The respondents conceded removability and did not seek any relief so the Immigration Judge ordered them removed.
 
The respondents appealed and on March 27, 2006, the Board dismissed the respondents’ appeals. The respondents filed a timely petition for review. On August 10, 2011, the Ninth Circuit dismissed the petition for review. Chee et al. v. Holder, No. 06-71952 (9th Cir. Aug. 8, 2011). The Ninth Circuit held that equitable estoppel against the government was not available where the respondents did not lose any rights to which they were entitled. The Ninth Circuit held that the respondents’ remaining contentions were not persuasive.
 
After receiving the 9th Circuit’s decision, the respondents hired new counsel and on November 17, 2011, they filed a motion to reopen our March 2006 decision. The motion is untimely. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
In the motion, the lead male respondent (AXXX XXX XXX) and his wife (AXXX XXX XXX) request that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. Their children (AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX), as well as AXXX XXX XXX on their behalf, contend that their former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel because they were unaware that they qualified for post-conclusion voluntary departure, and had they known that they qualified, they would have insisted that their former counsel request it, rather than withdraw the request. In addition, AXXX XXX XXX seeks sua sponte reopening so that he may pursue adjustment of status based on an pending I-130 visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the motion.
 
The motion will be denied as untimely. The respondents AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX do not contend that ineffective assistance by former counsel is apparent on the face of the record. As such, the respondents had to substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondents attached a complaint and cover letter, but they did not submit sufficient evidence demonstrating that a complaint was filed with the appropriate bar office. The respondents did not attach, or supplement the record, with the certified mail receipt, the return receipt, or an acknowledgment letter from the state bar.
 
*2 Furthermore, even assuming substantial compliance with Lozada, the respondents have not demonstrated that former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondents claim that they were not aware of their eligibility for voluntary departure, and that they would have requested such relief had they been aware. Such an assertion comes 5 years after the Board dismissed the appeal. In light of the record and former counsel’s response, see DHS Opposition, Exh. A, their assertions are inherently not believable. Former counsel states that the respondents AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX did not want to pursue voluntary departure because they wanted to stay and apply for relief (DHS Opposition, Exh. A).
 
According to the transcript of proceedings, former counsel declined voluntary departure on the respondents’ behalf and did not seek any other applications for relief such as adjustment of status based on their labor because they were not eligible for adjustment at the time, but they hoped to seek relief in the future (Tr. at 147-48). Respondents, by their own admissions, were present during this discussion. Further, the respondents did not have a basis for asylum, withholding or protection under the Convention Against Torture (Tr. at 149). Their former counsel’s strategic decisions did not constitute ineffective assistance, but rather a strategic decision based on his understanding of the respondents’ wishes during the proceedings. Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096, 1101 (9th Cir. 2009) (care must be taken to not distort a lawyer’s decisions with the benefit of hindsight).
 
Given the respondents’ (AXXX XXX XXX and AXXX XXX XXX) desire to remain in the United States and their hope to adjust their status, it was a reasonable litigation tactic to withdraw the pending voluntary departure requests as a failure to comply with the stringent time constraints of voluntary departure would result in a bar on adjustment, among other restrictions, for 10 years. Section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d).
 
The respondents AXXX XXX XXX, AXXX XXX XXX, and AXXX XXX XXX seek reopening as a matter of discretion. We decline to exercise our sua sponte discretion to reopen because the respondents have not demonstrated exceptional circumstances. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Becoming eligible for adjustment subsequent to a final administrative order of removal is not unusual and is not by itself an exceptional circumstance.
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANA RAQUEL MEDRANO-MARTE A.K.A. ANA MEDRANO
File: AXXX XX1 222 - Boston, MA
May 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, who was previously granted lawful permanent resident status in the United States, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 2, 2012. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and found her ineligible for relief from removal based on her criminal convictions. Removability is not at issue on appeal. On appeal, the respondent challenges the denial of a continuance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent requested a continuance to collaterally attack the conviction pursuant to Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. __, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010),1 but the Immigration Judge denied that request (Tr. at 9-10; I.J. at 3). On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have granted her a continuance to pursue post-conviction relief. The respondent alleges that she was not advised of the immigration consequences of his conviction, and she has filed a motion to vacate it. However, the conviction has not been vacated.
 
An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. See also Matter of Rajah, 25 I&N Dec. 127 (BIA 2009); Matter of Hashmi, 24 I&N Dec. 785 (BIA 2009).2 We find that the Immigration Judge appropriately considered the relevant factors to determine whether good cause for a continuance was shown. We conclude that good cause was not shown.
 
While the respondent is free to pursue the post-conviction motion, such a motion does not justify a stay of the removal proceedings against the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). The post-conviction motion to vacate the conviction does not render the conviction non-final. See Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n. 8 (BIA 2009); Matter of Madrigal, supra; Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In Padilla v. Kentucky, the Supreme Court held that the failure to advise a non-citizen criminal defendant that a plea could result in deportation constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and violates the right to counsel.

2

In determining whether good cause exists to continue removal proceedings, a variety of factors may be considered, including, but not limited to: the Department of Homeland Security’s response to the motion to continue and the reason for the continuance and any other relevant procedural factors. See Matter of Hashmi, supra.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSUE ELIAS MUNGUIA-SALAZAR
File: AXXX XX4 461 - New Orleans, LA
May 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
 
 
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent has filed an appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 24, 2012, decision granting him voluntary departure. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The hearing transcript reveals that the respondent, through counsel, requested only the relief of pre-conclusion voluntary departure for a period of 90 days (Tr. at 7-10), a grant of which requires that the alien waive “appeal of all issues.” Section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D); see also Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (holding that 120 days’ voluntary departure may not be granted prior to the completion of removal proceedings without an express waiver of the right to appeal). The transcript further reflects that, after explaining the order, the Immigration Judge asked the respondent’s counsel whether the respondent waived appeal, to which the respondent’s counsel responded “He does, Your Honor.” (Tr. at 10, lines 9-12).
 
On February 23, 2012, the respondent nevertheless filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), contending that he was not advised during the proceedings that he was required to waive his appeal rights in order to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure; and he did not receive an adequate explanation regarding the ramifications of waiving appeal. However, as set out above, the respondent, with counsel present, was explicitly advised that he was required to waive his appeal rights in order to accept pre-conclusion voluntary departure. See Kohwarienv. Holder, 635F. 3d 174 (5th Cir. 2011) (finding that an alien accepting pre-conclusion voluntary departure knowingly and intelligently waived his appellate rights). To the extent the respondent’s appeal can be construed as alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, he has complied with none of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making such a claim. See also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006).
 
Consequently, the respondent, through counsel, knowingly and intelligently waived appeal. The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and that decision remains the final administrative order in these proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) (final sentence), 1003.39 (finality of order); Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
