IN RE: ABDEL RAZEK SAAD ZAGHLOUL AHMED
File: AXXX XX6 197 - San Antonio, TX
May 31, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ismael Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Claire W. Matecko
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Egypt, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated February 28, 2011, denying his motion to reconsider. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
The respondent was ordered removed in absentia by an Immigration Judge on October 6, 1998. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(6)(A). On October 27, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Court. On December 2, 2010, the Immigration Court mailed the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated December 1, 2010, denying the motion to reopen to the parties. On January 4, 2011, 33 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the motion to reopen was mailed, the respondent filed a “Motion to Reconsider Reopening Removal Proceedings” wherein he “move[d] for reconsideration of his motion to reopen removal proceedings” (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 2).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The Immigration Judge properly recognized that the respondent’s motion to reconsider was untimely as it was filed more than 30 days after the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen (I.J. at 2). See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent does not dispute that his motion to reconsider was untimely. See Claudio v. Holder, 601 F.3d 316, 319 (5th Cir. 2010) (holding that once an alien elects in his notice of appeal to file a brief, that brief becomes the operative document through which any issues that the alien wishes to have considered must be raised). While the respondent does claim that his removal would result in hardship to his family and he has other equities in the United States, the power to reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). As such, sua sponte reconsideration was not warranted.
 
On appeal, the respondent attempts to challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. For example, he argues that the untimeliness of his motion to reopen was justified, that his motion to reopen complied with the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and that he is eligible to adjust status under section 245(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i) (Respondent’s Br. at 1-5).1 However, the respondent did not appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen. As such, any arguments concerning the merits of the motion to reopen are not properly before us. Instead, the only decision currently before us for the purposes of appellate review is the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated February 28, 2011, which properly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider as untimely. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not allege that the attorney who filed the motion to reconsider provided ineffective assistance of counsel by not timely filing the motion or allege that he has fulfilled the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, in order to properly raise such a claim.

2012 WL 2252948 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO CABALLERO-GUAPILLA A.K.A JUAN JOSE CABALLERO A.K.A MIGUEL ANGEL LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX9 636 - Aurora, CO
May 30, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Evelyn G. Zneimer, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 On June 10, 2010, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent a period of voluntary departure in lieu of removal, and the respondent waived appeal. The Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen proceedings on June 21, 2011. On March 7, 2011, the Immigration Judge denied a motion for reconsideration of the denial of reopening, considering relevant case law and allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent’s appeal of the denial of reconsideration was received on April 9, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal.
 
The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on March 7, 2012. The appeal was accordingly due on or before April 6, 2012. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on April 9, 2012. We find that the appeal is untimely, and the respondent has provided no explanation for the untimeliness of the appeal. The Immigration Judge’s decision is accordingly now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15 (1999).
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 David B. Holmes

2012 WL 2252972 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BALAKRISHNA HEGDE
File: AXXX XX1 931 - New York, NY
May 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Parker Waggaman, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of India, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on April 27, 2010, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on May 27, 2010, that was denied by an Immigration Judge on July 7, 2010. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen on February 9, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied it on February 24, 2012, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). These proceedings fall within the REAL ID Act.
 
The respondent’s second motion to reopen is untimely. A request for rescission of an order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Because the second request for reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was not made within 180 days of the date of the respondent’s order of removal, it is barred from consideration. The respondent also does not assert that he was not notified of his hearing.
 
We note that the respondent argued in his first motion, through a former attorney, that he was unable to attend his April 27, 2010, hearing, because he was in India. The respondent asserts on appeal that he was not outside the United States, and it constituted ineffective assistance of counsel for his former attorney to have made that assertion in his first motion. However, the respondent does not explain either on appeal or in his motions his reason for not attending his hearing. He therefore would not have established that “exceptional circumstances” or lack of notice prevented his appearance. We thus do not find that it would be warranted to reopen these proceedings even assuming that his second motion to reopen was filed timely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS HUIPIO DELGADO
File: AXXX XX0 124 - Houston, TX
May 18, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven P. Mock, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wm. Tracy Hamby
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated January 11, 2010, denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
A motion to reopen must state the new facts that will be proven at the reopened hearing and be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials demonstrating prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). An alien who seeks to remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a heavy burden of proving that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. See Matter of Coelho, supra.
 
For the first time on appeal, the respondent alleges that an individual who is not a member of the State Bar of Texas purported to represent him at a scheduling hearing, and for that reason, his counsel of record was uninformed concerning the next pertinent date in the proceedings, causing him to miss a critical deadline. First, the respondent himself was present at the hearing where the Immigration Judge provided the date at issue (Tr. at 9-10). The heavy burden for prevailing on a motion to reopen cannot be shifted away from the respondent due to his failure to relate this information to his counsel of record. Next, the respondent made no effort to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), with respect to any ineffective assistance of counsel claim he intended to pursue. Finally, respondent’s counsel of record was on notice, due to the lapse of time, that some intervening court action might have occurred, but failed to investigate. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reopen warrants denial, as he did not establish that new information affected the outcome of his case. Matter of Coelho, supra
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 8305101 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO ISMAEL DIAZ-BISONO
File: AXXX XX7 740 - Atlanta, GA
May 16, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sarah Owings, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joy Lampley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent is a citizen of the Dominican Republic. On November 22, 2011, an Immigration Judge granted him the opportunity to depart the United States voluntarily by January 23, 2012, under safeguards. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on January 17, 2012. The Immigration Judge denied it on February 27, 2012, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). These proceedings fall within the REAL ID Act.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen. He disputes the Immigration Judge’s finding that he did not substantially comply with the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Dakane v. U.S. Atty.Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005).
 
Even assuming that the respondent satisfied the procedural requirements for his claim, he must also establish that his former counsel’s performance was deficient to the point that it impinged on the fundamental fairness of the hearing. See Ali v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011). It is required that he establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s alleged ineffectiveness. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not established that he was prima facie eligible for a grant of any form of relief from removal at his hearing other than voluntary departure. In fact, the respondent does not even address the issue with particularity in either his brief or the Notice of Appeal. Instead, the respondent’s contentions are limited to whether he satisfied the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. He has therefore not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s representation.
 
*2 We acknowledge that the respondent asserted in his motion to reopen that his former counsel’s inaction deprived him of the opportunity to be considered at the hearing for a “stand alone” waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1182(h) (See motion at 5). However, he is not eligible to be granted the waiver.
 
In Matter of Abosi and Matter of Sanchez, the Board found that an alien could apply for a “stand alone” 212(h) waiver, where the alien was a returning legal permanent resident seeking to overcome a ground of inadmissibility. However, an alien, like the respondent, who is within the United States and charged with deportability must submit an application for a 212(h) waiver concurrently with an application for a visa, admission, or adjustment of status. See section 212(h)(2) of the Act (a waiver of inadmissibility is permitted where the ““““Attorney General ... has consented to the alien’s applying or reapplying for a visa, for admission to the United States, or adjustment of status); 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(f) (“an application under this part shall be the sole method of requesting the exercise of discretion under sections 212(g), (h), (i), and (k) as they relate to the inadmissibility of an alien in the United States”).
 
The respondent is not an alien seeking admission into the United States and would not be applying for the waiver of inadmissibility in conjunction was an application for adjustment of status. In fact, the respondent acknowledges in his motion that he could not adjust his status to a lawful permanent resident of the United States in the foreseeable future because of his visa priority date. We do not find that the respondent established that he was denied a fair hearing warranting reopening of these proceedings. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: CHUKWUNMA OKOENE

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniel P. Hanlon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 By a motion filed on December 6, 2011, the respondent seeks to have the proceedings reopened based upon his assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
An alien’s right to due process can be violated by “egregious conduct that threatens the fairness of the proceeding,” including egregiously deficient performance by the alien’s lawyer. Torres-Chavez v. Holder, 567 F.3d 1096 (9th Cir. 2009), citing Nehad v. Mukasey, 535 F.3d 962, 967, 971 (9th Cir. 2008); accord Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 933 (9th Cir.1986). Therefore, in assessing an attorney’s performance, “[t]he proper focus of our inquiry is whether the proceeding is so fundamentally unfair that the alien is prevented from reasonably presenting her case.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 974 (9th Cir. 2004) (citation and internal quotation marks omitted). An alien must also show “substantial prejudice” by demonstrating that “the alleged violation affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Id. at 973; see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec.553, 560 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent’s assertion of ineffective assistance is lodged against an attorney who was disbarred in 2010, Anita C. Kanu. A review of the transcript of proceedings raises serious concerns as to the representation afforded the respondent by that attorney. See Tr. at pp.40, 43, 55; I.J. Dec. at p. 2. Further, the respondent’s affidavit, which we accept as true for purposes of this motion (see Najmabadi v. Holder, 597 F.3d 983, 994 (9th Cir. 2010)), details that his former attorney continued to mishandle his case resulting in the decision of the Board of December 29, 2006, dismissing the respondent’s appeal and denying his motion to remand, noting that, “[w]hile the respondent has submitted a copy of his marriage certificate, the respondent has failed to provide proof that his United States citizen spouse filed a visa petition (Form I-130) on his behalf, submit an application for adjustment of status (Form I-485), or provide evidence establishing the bona fides of his marriage.” Further, the record reflects that Ms. Kanu improperly filed an application for suspension of deportation in these removal proceedings, and the Immigration Judge would have entertained a motion to reopen to apply for cancellation of removal, but no such motion was filed. Following the Board’s decision, the respondent continued to pursue his case with other representatives, ultimately learning of the extent of the mishandling of his proceedings by Ms. Kanu.
 
*2 We find that the respondent relied on the prior attorney to his detriment. Given the evidence of record and the detailed arguments presented in support of the motion, we find that an adequate showing of due diligence has been made so as to warrant equitable tolling and the reopening of these proceedings. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
Therefore, we will grant the respondent’s unopposed motion to reopen and we will remand the record to the Immigration Court so that the respondent may be afforded a hearing on any application for relief that may be available to him. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark

IN RE: SUDEEN ANIKA BLIDGEN A.K.A. SHAVON R. MUSE

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniel E. Marcus, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 1, 2011. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under a clearly erroneous standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 637 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion for a continuance to seek post-conviction relief.1 The Immigration Judge properly denied the request for failure to demonstrate good cause because the outcome of any efforts to obtain post-conviction relief, including a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. ___, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010), would be speculative, and the respondent’s conviction is considered final for immigration purposes unless and until it is overturned. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987) (the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240; Matter of Abreu, 24 I&N Dec. 795, 802 n.8 (BIA 2009) (“A pending collateral attack does not disturb the finality of a conviction and therefore would not justify reopening of removal proceedings.”); Matter of Adetiba, 20 I&N Dec. 506, 508 (BIA 1992) (“the possibility of a decision on any post-conviction motion that has been filed does not affect our finding that the respondent is deportable.”); see also Lanferman v. BIA, 576 F.3d 84, 88 (2d Cir. 2009) (a collateral attack on alien’s state court conviction is not available a petition for review of the Board’s removal order).2
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Sharon Hoffman
FOR THE BOARD

However, the term “collateral attack” in this context is used in its legal sense, i.e., an attempt to attack the validity of a judgment in a proceeding other than a direct appeal.

IN RE: TOMASZ JAN DIDENKO JOLANTA BRONISLAWA DIDENKO
Files: AXXX XX2 180 - Memphis, TN
AXXX XX2 179
May 3, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Agnieszka Haupt, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scot A. Bearup
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondents, both natives and citizens of Poland, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 20, 2010, which denied their request for a continuance but granted voluntary departure under section 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. The request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but we review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). Upon de novo review, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance. Although the respondents argue on appeal that there was good cause to grant a continuance based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel in visa petition proceedings, they have not filed, either before the Immigration Judge or the Board, any documents showing that they are pursuing the ineffective assistance claim or that such a claim is factually supported. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In any event, inasmuch as the alleged ineffective assistance relates to an employment-based visa petition, such matter is outside the scope of the Immigration Judge’s and the Board’s jurisdiction. See Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 339 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(b), 1240.1(a)(1).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that any possible favorable change in immigration law is speculative. Moreover, to date, the respondents have not filed an application for relief or otherwise identified any relief from removal for which they may be eligible. Thus, the respondents have not shown that there was good cause for a continuance or that the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance resulted in actual prejudice. Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356 (BIA 1983); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. See Al-Ghorbani v. Holder, 585 F.3d 980, 992 (6th Cir. 2009). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fail to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondents file a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondents file any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondents file a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).

2012 WL 1705582 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABUNDIO PEREZ-CORBALAT
File: AXXX XX9 712 - San Diego, CA
April 26, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Barbara K. Strickland, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kori LaPoint
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Guatemala who was ordered removed in absentia by an Immigration Judge on November 4, 2010, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated July 6, 2011, denying his motion to reopen these removal proceedings, which was filed with the Immigration Judge on May 2, 2011. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely under the general 90-day rule governing motions to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i). Nonetheless, an order of removal entered in absentia can be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of “exceptional circumstances.” See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. In removal proceedings, the term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances (such as battery or extreme cruelty to the alien or any child or parent of the alien, serious illness of the alien, or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.1 Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
We agree with the respondent that the reasonableness of an alien’s cause for not appearing at his hearing is not dispositive as to whether an in absentia order of removal should be rescinded under the provisions of section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. Instead, the question that must be resolved is whether the alien has demonstrated that the reasons for his failure to appear amount to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i), (e)(1) of the Act.
 
In this case, the respondent argues that he made a “mistake” and a “mundane misunderstanding” and was “confused” regarding the date of his removal hearing. Even though the Notice of Hearing advised that the respondent’s “case has been scheduled for a[n] individual hearing before the Immigration Court on Nov 4, 2010,” it appears that the respondent argues that he was confused as the hearing notice was issued on December 23, 2009, and he believed that his hearing was scheduled for December 2010. However, the Immigration Judge properly recognized that such claims did not amount to exceptional circumstances warranting rescission of the order of removal entered against the respondent. See Valencia-Fragoso v. INS, 321 F.3d 1204, 1205-06 (9th Cir. 2003) (per curiam) (finding no exceptional circumstances where the alien misunderstood the time of her hearing). The respondent’s claims are far less compelling than battery, extreme cruelty, serious illness, or death. See section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent attempts to place blame on an employee of his attorney’s law office, we observe that the respondent has explicitly declined to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Respondent’s Br. at 2). Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated that the ability to properly understand the Notice of Hearing, despite incorrect information from his attorney’s law office, was beyond his control. In any event, his claims are not sufficiently compelling to warrant rescission of the Immigration Judge’s order of removal
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that he is eligible for relief under special rule cancellation of removal pursuant to section 203 of the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act, Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193, 2096, amended by Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644(1997) (“NACARA”), we observe that the record does not contain any evidence that he has filed an application for that form of relief. Thus, there is no support in the record for the respondent’s claims that “it is exceedingly likely that he would have prevailed on his NACARA special rule cancellation application” (Respondent’s Br. at 5). He makes no argument concerning the viability of his application for asylum. Moreover, the plain language of section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act requires that “exceptional circumstances” caused the alien’s failure to appear. A mere showing of generalized “exceptional circumstances” unrelated to the alien’s reasons for failing to appear at his removal hearing does not warrant rescission of a removal order under the provisions of section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act.2 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The aliens described in Singh v. INS, 295 F.3d 1037 (9th Cir. 2002), Chete Juarez v. Ashcroft, 376 F.3d 944 (9th Cir. 2004), and Matter of A-N- & R-M-N-, 22 I&N Dec. 953 (BIA 1999), were in deportation proceedings. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit concluded that the original opinion in Vukmirovic v. Holder, 621 F.3d 1043 (9th Cir. 2010), “constituted a departure from Ninth Circuit precedent and interpreted too broadly the ‘exceptional circumstances’ safe harbor for aliens removed in absentia.” Vukmirovic v. Holder, 640 F.3d 977, 978 (9th Cir. 2011).

2

The respondent does not allege that the Immigration Judge should have reopened these proceedings under his sua sponte authority. Even if such claim was made, we would conclude that sua sponte reopening is not warranted. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).

2012 WL 1705613 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO ANTONIO FUENTES-CHICAS
File: AXXX XX7 438 - Houston, TX
April 26, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se1
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret R. Kugel
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 Before us for decision is the respondent’s motion to reopen seeking an opportunity to apply for special rule cancellation of removal under section 203 of the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act, Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193, 2196, amended by Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644 (1997) (NACARA). The motion, which is opposed by the DHS, will be denied.
 
The instant motion (filed December 1, 2011), is untimely by more than 9 years. Section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); August 9, 2002, Board dec. (final administrative decision).2 There also has been no showing that at the late filing of this motion is excused. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i)-(iv).
 
In addition, we decline to reopen the respondent’s proceedings sua sponte, inasmuch as he has not demonstrated the existence of exceptional circumstances warranting such action. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999). Further, notwithstanding the currently-advanced assertions to the contrary, the respondent, a citizen of El Salvador, has not shown prima facie eligibility for special rule cancellation of removal.3 He entered the United States in March 1985, and therefore is an “ABC class member.” See American Baptist Churches, et al. v. Thornburgh, et al., 760 F. Supp. 796 (N.D. Cal. 1991) (ABC); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.60. However, there has been no additional evidentiary showing that he is a “registered ABC class member,” as defined in 8 C.F.R. § 1240.60 (requiring a person, such as the respondent, to have registered for ABC benefits with the former INS, or to have applied for Temporary Protected Status (TPS),4 on or before October 31, 1991). As such, he does not qualify as a section 203 NACARA principal applicant under 8 C.F.R. § 1240.61(a)(1). Further, the respondent has not established that he applied for asylum or before April 1, 1990, such that he qualifies as a principal applicant for such relief under 8 C.F.R. § 1240.61(a)(2).
 
*2 Insofar as it is also asserted that the respondent received ineffective assistance by his former attorney, this allegation has been advanced only by Mr. Chukwurah, not by the respondent himself. See Respondent’s Response to Government’s Opposition to Motion to Reopen at 2. Moreover, in moving to reopen the respondent has not submitted any evidence reflecting his compliance with the requirements for presenting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, as delineated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. The request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

As Mr. Chukwurah is not permitted to practice law before the Board at this time, today’s order is being sent directly to the respondent, with a copy being provided to Mr. Chukwurah. Please also see the attached copy of the order suspending Mr. Chukwurah from practice.

2012 WL 1705600 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO GUTIERREZ A.K.A. LEONARDO GUITERREZ FLORES
File: AXXX XX9 223 - San Antonio, TX
April 25, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 This case was last before us on March 21, 2011, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s September 28, 2010, denial of his request for a continuance. The respondent has now filed an untimely motion to reopen proceedings on December 27, 2011. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel in his previous proceedings because his counsel before the Immigration Judge failed to timely apply for withholding of removal, failed to obtain a bond for the respondent, and failed to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision. He further argues that his attorney before the Board was not licensed as an attorney in the state of Texas, and also argues that she failed to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim during the appeal process. In support of his argument, the respondent has submitted an affidavit, a copy of the client agreement between himself and Ronald Higgins, Esquire, and evidence that he has filed complaints against both of his prior attorneys. The respondent has also filed evidence that he has pursued post-collateral relief regarding his conviction.
 
Notwithstanding this evidence, we do not find that the respondent has demonstrated prejudice as a result of his attorneys’ actions. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has not submitted evidence that he contracted with his first attorney to represent him on appeal, and inasmuch as this Board accepted the respondent’s untimely brief and considered his appeal on the merits, we do not find that he was prejudiced by any change in representation. Moreover, the respondent has not submitted an agreement with his prior appellate counsel.
 
Further, the respondent has failed to file an application for withholding of removal, as required by the regulations governing motions to reopen, nor has he set forth any kind of persecution claim. See § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent has not shown that he was prejudiced by his first attorney’s failure to pursue withholding of removal on his behalf, or by his appellate attorney’s failure to raise his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent has also not demonstrated how he was affected by his appellate attorney’s licensure in New York rather than Texas, and the respondent has submitted insufficient evidence regarding the nature of his representation during his bond proceedings. Lastly, we note that while the respondent has proffered documentation that he has filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus with the United States District Court, Middle District of Florida, Orlando Division, he has not submitted any indication that the Court changed the disposition of his conviction in alignment with Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003); see also Matter of Chavez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007).
 
*2 Accordingly, we find no basis upon which to grant the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen proceedings based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Matter of Lozada, supra; Matter of Assaad, supra. The motion will, thus, be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

2012 WL 1705618 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICIO CLODOMIRO VELEZ-DELGADO
File: AXXX XX8 502 - Newark, NJ
April 23, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Teodora Arias, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia M. Medeiros
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 On March 15, 2012, the respondent submitted an untimely motion to reopen proceedings in which his appeal was dismissed by the Board on March 28, 2008. He also seeks a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes reopening. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent asks that he be considered for “prosecutorial discretion” pursuant to the “Morton Memorandum” of November 17, 2011 (Motion at 3). He further contends that his attorney, Mr. Pollack, did not inform him of the Board’s adverse decision. However, the respondent has not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor has he made any showing of prejudice, and reopening on this basis is not warranted. Rranci v. Att’y Gen., 540 F.3d 165, 172 (3d Cir. 2008) (outlining those requirements); Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 129 (3d Cir. 2001) (holding that “the Lozada requirements are a reasonable exercise of the Board’s discretion”).
 
The respondent further avers he is a “perfect candidate for prosecutorial discretion” as outlined in the “Morton Memorandum” (Motion at 3). However, this Board is not authorized to take action on such a request. Rather, a request for the favorable exercise of prosecutorial discretion can be directed to the DHS. While the respondent also cites humanitarian factors in asking that proceedings be reopened, these factors do not constitute an exception to the statutory time limitation.
 
As the respondent has not demonstrated that his motion is exempt from the time limits on motions to reopen, neither reopening nor a stay of removal is warranted. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Danny C. Campbell, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)] - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Vietnam, has appealed from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated June 18, 2010. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s June 7, 2010, motion to reopen, determining that there is “no legal basis” upon which to reopen the proceedings (I.J.). We agree. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for the reasons stated therein. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5) (2011). We write separately for two reasons. First, to the extent the respondent has accused her prior counsel of providing her with bad legal advice, she has not made an effective allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel; she has not complied with the standards for such claims set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir.1988). Finally, although the respondent has claimed that her removal will result in “extreme hardship,” she has not supported the motion to reopen with any application for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley

2012 WL 1705614 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BEATRICE AKUMU ONONO
File: AXXX XX7 955 - Baltimore, MD
April 20, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven P. Barsamian, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. In a decision issued May 9, 2000, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent filed a motion to reopen. She conceded that she had notice of the hearing, but averred that she was unable to travel due to illness. The Immigration Judge denied the motion because the respondent had not demonstrated exceptional circumstances. The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal, and on April 20, 2001, the Board dismissed the appeal.
 
Over 11 years later, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. She alleges that the time within which to reopen should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel. She also seeks reopening under the Board’s sua sponte discretionary authority. The motion to reopen is denied as untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
The respondent has not satisfied the requirements of equitable tolling. She has not demonstrated substantial compliance with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 745-46 (4th Cir. 2006). Nor has the respondent demonstrated that she exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. See Chao v. Virginia Department of Transportation, 291 F.3d 276, 283 (4th Cir. 2002)(equitable tolling is not available when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf).
 
The limited evidence proffered with the motion, considered with the other evidence of record, does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JEFFRY DEJESUS GONZALEZ PENA A.K.A. JEFFREY GONZALEZ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This case was previously before the Board on February 27, 2012, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s November 3, 2011, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed from the United States. On March 15, 2012, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider and reopen with the Board alleging that his former counsel did not file an appellate brief on his behalf.1 The respondent also claims that his current counsel will file a motion to withdraw his guilty plea with the Lawrence District Court. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a brief in opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.2
 
To the extent the motion is a motion to reconsider, it does not specify errors of fact or law in our former decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (stating that a motion to reconsider shall specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority.”); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). To the extent the motion is a motion to reopen, it is not supported with the requisite evidence. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (stating that a motion to reopen “shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.”). The respondent’s counsel’s statements in the brief are not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); Matter of S-M-, 22 I&N Dec. 49, 51 (BIA 1998). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider and reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes

2012 WL 1705670 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: COMFORT AFUA AFARBEA KOLI A.K.A COMFORT AFARIBEA WOODS
File: AXXX XX3 114 - Cleveland, OH
April 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Floyd J. Fernandez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 On June 22, 2010, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind an order of removal entered in absentia on July 26, 2007. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent claimed that she did not receive proper notice of her removal hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). On April 29, 2011, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision, noting that the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) was sent to the respondent at the address she provided. On November 2, 2011, the respondent filed the instant untimely motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(i). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent, who sought and continues to seek adjustment of status as the spouse of a United States citizen, raises ineffective assistance of counsel claims in the instant motion. Although she alleges that her former attorneys performed deficiently in both visa petition and removal proceedings, we emphasize that visa petition proceedings are separate and distinct from these removal proceedings. The dispositive issue now before the Board is whether ineffective assistance of counsel prevented the respondent from attending the scheduled removal hearing on July 26, 2007.
 
An alien may move to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal at any time if she did not receive proper notice. See section 240(c)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. An alien may also move to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal within 180 days after date of the in absentia order if she establishes that the failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances. See section 240(c)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. The respondent now seeks to toll the filing deadline in light of her claim that she did not receive the NTA due to ineffective assistance of counsel.1 See Motion to Reopen at 3.
 
The respondent claims that her former attorney, Rateb Khasawneh, received a copy of the NTA in May 2006, and that he failed to notify her of the scheduled removal hearing. Id., at 6-7. She also alleges that Emmanuel Olawale, Esquire, the attorney who filed the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge and the appeal before the Board, failed to raise the issue of Rateb Khasawneh’s alleged failure regarding the NTA. See Motion Exhibit A-2, at 2-3.
 
Initially, we find that the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel before this Board as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). See Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 725, 727 (6th Cir. 2007) (“An alien who fails to comply with Lozada’s requirements forfeits her ineffective-assistance-of-counsel claim”). We find no evidence of Rateb Khasawneh’s representation of the respondent in the record, and we find no evidence of the respondent’s compliance with Lozada’s requirements with regard to her claim of ineffective of counsel against him. With regard to her claim against Attorney Olawale, the respondent has not offered evidence that she filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities against Attorney Olawale in compliance with Lozada’s requirements. We further note that in his response to the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, Attorney Olawale explains that he learned from the respondent after the dismissal of her appeal that Rateb Khasawneh had received a copy of the NTA. Attorney Olawale disputes the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 In sum, the respondent has not set forth viable claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Further, even if the respondent had complied with Lozada’s requirements, and even if she had shown (which she has not) that she suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel, equitable tolling is not appropriate because the respondent has not shown that she has acted with due diligence in bringing her ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board. See Barry v. Mukasey, 524 F.3d 721, 724 (6th Cir. 2008). We note that in making her initial motion to reopen to rescind, no claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was made.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall-Clark
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent concedes that she was no longer living at her address of record at the time the NTA was mailed to her. See Respondent’s Motion at 4-5. She specifically claims that she did not receive the NTA because she had moved. Id. Although she states that she notified the U.S. Postal Service that she had moved, the respondent has neither claimed nor provided evidence that she notified the Department of Homeland Security of her change of address.

2012 WL 1705669 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFONSO GASPAR-VELARDE A.K.A. ALFONSO GASPAR-VELARDO A.K.A. JOSE GASPAR-BELARDO
File: AXXX XX6 001 - Miami, FL
April 11, 2012
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Andrew Brown
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico who entered the United States without inspection by an immigration officer, appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated January 10, 2012, ordering his removal from this country. We will dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
It is undisputed that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States as a result of his manner of entry into this country (I.J. at 2-3; Exh. 1).1 See section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i). As the respondent is subject to removal, it is his burden to establish eligibility for relief from removal. See section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). On appeal, the respondent does not allege that he is eligible for any form of relief from removal that can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to enter an order of removal in this matter in lieu of further continuing proceedings. An Immigration Judge may grant a continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. An Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance will not be overturned unless the respondent establishes that the denial deprived him of his due process right to a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Luviano, 21 I&N Dec. 235, 237 (BIA 1996) (citing Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987)).
 
In this case, the respondent was personally served with a Notice to Appear on June 17, 2011 (Exh. 1). The respondent was granted continuances on July 21, 2011 (Tr. at 2), August 22, 2011 (Tr. at 4, 9-10), November 3, 2011 (Tr. at 12), November 29, 2011 (Tr. at 13), and January 3, 2012 (Tr. at 15). Additionally, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s written motion for a continuance in October 2011.
 
Despite having been afforded a significant amount of time to prepare for his removal proceedings, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have further continued removal proceedings as he desires to apply for a U visa with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”) under the provisions of section 101(a)(15)(U) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(15)(U), he had recently hired an unnamed “new attorney,” and that his prior counsel was ineffective. The respondent argues that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance by not appearing at scheduled hearings and not properly presenting and pursuing his claim for a U visa.2
 
*2 USCIS has “sole jurisdiction” over all applications for U visa status and an alien who is subject to a final order of removal is not precluded from filing such an application. 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1). Additionally, 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(1)(ii) explicitly provides that, while the pendency of an application for a U visa has no effect on the Government’s authority to execute a removal order, an applicant for a U visa is permitted to file a request for a stay of removal with the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”). If USCIS approves the respondent’s application, he may file a motion to reopen and terminate removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(5)(i). Even if the respondent departs the United States, he may also pursue his application from abroad. 8 C.F.R. §214.14(c)(5)(i)(B). Considering the significant number of procedural safeguards which are in place in order to allow an alien a reasonable opportunity to pursue a U visa subsequent to an order of removal being entered against him, as well as the significant amount of time that was already provided to the respondent to pursue a U visa, we do not consider the speculative possibility that the respondent will be granted a U visa to be good cause to further continue these proceedings.3 See generally Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 110 (BIA 2009) (“As a practical matter, Immigration Judges and the Board have limited and finite adjudicative and administrative resources, and those resources are best allocated to matters over which we do have jurisdiction.”).
 
While the respondent claims that he had recently hired new counsel, he does not state the name of the representative that he allegedly hired, he has not presented any evidence to support such claims, and has not explained why these removal proceedings should have been continued in order for this unnamed attorney to enter an appearance. As discussed above, the respondent does not dispute that he is subject to removal from the United States and does not allege eligibility for any form of relief from removal that can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board. As such, the respondent’s claims concerning a “new attorney” do not establish that a further continuance was warranted. Such claims are also insufficient to establish that the unnamed attorney was ineffective.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues that his former counsel was ineffective, we observe that the respondent has not presented (1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with his former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) evidence that his former counsel has been informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) any indication as to whether a complaint against his former counsel has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*3 Additionally, the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel do not amount to a basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision as the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his counsel’s conduct. See Ali v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 643 F.3d 1324, 1329 (11th Cir. 2011). Given that the respondent does not dispute that he is subject to removal from the United States and does not allege eligibility for any form of relief that can be granted by an Immigration Judge or this Board, the respondent has not demonstrated that a reasonable probability that but for the counsel’s alleged errors, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different. Even if an ineffective assistance claim could be premised on an counsel’s conduct regarding issues which are not directly related to removal proceedings, i.e., the pursuit of a U visa, the respondent has not demonstrated that an authorized official is willing to “certify” the respondent for the issuance of a U visa. See 8 C.F.R. § 214.14(c)(2)(i). The former counsel’s assertions indicate that he made efforts to procure a certification from the state attorney’s office in Orange County, Florida and that the office was unresponsive (Tr. at 13, 16). The mere fact that the state attorney’s office has declined to issue a certification to the respondent does not established that the respondent’s counsel was ineffective. The respondent has not established that, even his former counsel’s assistance was free from the alleged defects, there is a reasonable probability that the state attorney’s office would have issued the requisite certification.
 
On appeal, the respondent has presented documents relating to his pursuit of the requisite certification needed for a U visa. The Board does not consider evidence first provided on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). Additionally, we decline to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the evidence in the first instance as such evidence has not been shown to affect the outcome of this case. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). As discussed above, USCIS has sole jurisdiction to grant a U visa. Moreover, the evidence offered by the respondent does not demonstrate that an authorized official is willing to certify the respondent for U visa status. Accordingly, the evidence offer does not establish that the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent removed to Mexico should be disturbed. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Elise L. Manuel
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

As the respondent is subject to removal as a result of entering the United States without inspection by an immigration officer, we need not further address whether he is also subject to removal as a result of his criminal history.

2

The record reflects that the respondent’s former counsel did not timely appear at the removal hearing scheduled on August 22, 2011 (Tr. at 4). However, he appeared later that day (Tr. at 6). The respondent’s former counsel did not appear at the hearing on January 3, 2012 (Tr. at 15).
